==PAGE:1==
MASTER THESIS FALL 2022
Developing new managerial practices: A predictive model for
organizing hybrid work to improve fist time daily managers’ ability
to lead
Authors:
Astrid Helene Erecius,s171013
& Emma Jespersen,s174324
Supervisors:
Christine Ipsen & Kathrin Kirchner
Department:
Department of Technology, Management and Economics
Submission Date: February 19, 2023
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
There are many, and sometimes even conflicting, perceptions of the value of a hybrid work model.
Oftentimes, upper management and employees are not on the same page about the amount of
telework the organization should implement. This leaves the daily managers stuck in between.
This thesis focuses on the challenges experienced by first-time daily managers and aims to
provide a machine learning model as a solution tool for one of the identified key challenges.
When investigating the challenges, it is explored whether the type of hybrid work model in an
organization can explain the challenges experienced by first-time daily managers. The study is a
mixed methods study, where the challenges are explored using a qualitative cross-case study, and
quantitative methods are applied to create the predictive model for a solution tool. The primary
data for the qualitative study was collected using semi-structured interviews with seven first-time
daily managers from knowledge based organizations. The secondary quantitative data used for
the prediction model was collected by Epinion in 2021.
The content-analytic meta matrix from the qualitative analysis results in 29 challenges across the
cases, with four of them being experienced by interviewees from all types of hybrid work models
represented in the data. Results from the causal network analysis suggest that the challenges
experienced by first-time daily managers in knowledge based organizations may be explained
by the type of hybrid work model implemented in an organization. However, these findings are
not unambiguous but may instead be due to gender or other factors. To confirm or dismiss the
suggested findings, an analysis with a larger sample size is needed.
The key challenge chosen to solve was Customizing work arrangements for employees as this was
an organizational challenge not being influenced by other challenges. Using a machine learning
model, based on Epinion data, the thesis attempts to predict the number of days employees in
knowledge based organizations prefer to work from home, to create a management tool to solve
one of the key challenges found in the qualitative analysis. After optimizing the model using
over-sampling and hyperparameter tuning, the results are concluded to not satisfyingly solve
the identified challenge. Although the model is not suited as a solution tool, the quantitative
results still show that the atmosphere at home and missing employees are the factors that have
the highest impact when determining how many days per week employees prefer to work from
home.

1
INTRODUCTION
Master Thesis
1
Introduction
The COVID-19 pandemic shook every corner of the world and changed the professional scene
for knowledge workers in how they work and interact (Komm, Pollner, Schaninger, & Sikka,
2021). Everyone had to come to terms with a new way of living in both their personal and
professional lives with working from home (WFH) as a part of it.
In the wake of the pandemic, when the Danish authorities opened the offices and ended the
restrictions, knowledge workers were met with something new: The hybrid work model. Working
hybrid entails working both at the office and from home - when and how is not specified by
the term but can be done in numerous ways. According to Nielsen and B. Olsson (2021), the
number of expected working days from home has increased post corona. Danish employees who
have the possibility of WFH have reported that before the pandemic they worked from home an
average of 2.5 days a month, whereas after COVID-19 they now work an average of 4.3 days
from home a month (Nielsen & B. Olsson, 2021).
WFH has shown to be challenging for knowledge workers on factors such as social isolation,
work uncertainties, and home office constraints, etc. (Ipsen, van Veldhoven, Kirchner, & Hansen,
2021). On the other hand, WFH has opened the knowledge worker’s eyes toward the freedom and
flexibility this can enrich their lives with and cause a desire to improve their work-life balance
(WLB) (Ipsen et al., 2021; Microsoft, 2022). This way, the pandemic turned many employees
towards viewing the new work model - a hybrid work model - more as a requirement than a
privilege, so much that some employees have been "threatening to quit if required to return to
the office" (De Souza Santos & Ralph, 2022).
The demanding attitude from the lower levels of knowledge-based organizations pushes the
top management to transform the modern-day organization. Some organizations have met this
demand for a more flexible work model and are rethinking the role of the offices, while others
are more reluctant to implement a hybrid work model due to the organizational challenges it can
inflict (Kurkland & Bailey, 1999).
The current climate of conflicting desires regarding WHF policy leaves the professional world
divided, and in the middle of all this, the daily manager is being squeezed between keeping up
organization lines while meeting employee demands.
Daily managers are often new to management as they frequently are recruited from a specialist
role (Raulgaonkar, 2021, 3). First-time daily managers are those, who have become daily man-
agers for the first time. Not only are first-time daily managers required to settle into the role of
manager, but they are now challenged by navigating an unfamiliar, hybrid work environment —
often without the experience or competencies to do so (Kirchner, Ipsen, & Hansen, 2021).
This exploratory multi-case study investigates the challenges of managing hybrid teams based
Page 1 of 238
==PAGE:11==
1
INTRODUCTION
Master Thesis
on interviews with first-time daily managers from six knowledge-based organizations. Moreover,
the study investigates whether the type of hybrid work model chosen by a knowledge-based
organization affects the challenges met by first-time daily managers.
Focusing on the challenges of first-time daily managers, it is essential for knowledge-based
organizations that they, in some way, are able to relieve the managers from some of these
challenges. To this end, management tools can be applied. Proposed tools must be simple to
prevent further complications or intrusion of the already challenging tasks of first-time daily
managers of hybrid work. For some time, quantitative methods have been incorporated into the
management toolbox, as they can assist with valuable information to support planning, decision
making and risk management etc. (Jarke, 1988; Reshi & Khan, 2014). According to Saunders,
Lewis, and Thornhill (2007, p. 127), when discussing the pros and cons of combining inductive
and deductive research approaches, their experience is that "most managers are familiar with
deduction and much more likely to put faith in the conclusions emanating from this approach"
(Saunders et al., 2007, p. 127). Building on this argument, using quantitative methods to create a
tool for managers may be a way to get them to take the tool into use.
Quantitative methods can build on large amounts of data, following them to reach a wide group
of information channels quickly. Despite of quantitative methods’ advantages, they have often
been challenged in managerial contexts for being too complex or difficult to comprehend and
are therefore often dismissed by decision-makers (Jarke, 1988). For quantitative methods to gain
a footing in the corporate world, more simple and easy-to-use quantitative methods need to be in
place. One such method, which has shown to be welcomed, is artificial intelligence (AI).
In recent years, AI and machine learning have increased in popularity and gained a footing in
many organizations due to their reputation as forward-looking and promising tools. Greenstein
and Rao (2022) AI Business Survey of 1,000 US business and technology executives establishes
that AI can improve aspects like productivity, decision-making and employee experience within
organizations (Greenstein & Rao, 2022). PwC deems AI-supported decision-making powerful,
arguing that
"it can enable you to incorporate and analyze far more information than you (or any human
being) could do on their own" (Greenstein & Rao, 2022).
One application of AI is machine learning, which uses statistical methods to train algorithms to
make predictions (IBM, n.d.). Research shows that machine learning can be used in an organiza-
tional context to predict aspects like employee retention and stress (Jain, Jain, & Pamula, 2020;
Marvin, Jackson, & Alam, 2021; Reddy, Thota, & Dharun, 2018).
Page 2 of 238
==PAGE:12==
1
INTRODUCTION
Master Thesis
The high demand for the implementation of AI methods, such as machine learning, in various
areas of organizations has substantially increased the demand for technology related skills and
transformed the job market (Lyons, Kass-Hanna, Zucchetti, & Cobo, 2019, p. 2). An example is
its introduction into the corporate world as a management tool, where it can help tackle different
types of managerial challenges Kiwanuka, Karadsheh, Alqatawna, and Muhamad Amin (2021);
Reshi and Khan (2014). As a part of the G20 summit in 2019 in Japan, a report was conducted,
which accentuated the importance of digital literacy in workers and for this to be achieved only
through "a holistic framework encompassing technical and socioemotional dimensions" (Lyons
et al., 2019, p. 1). The conclusion made from the report is that it is a mistake leaving out the
human perspective in context with the use of data-driven models:
"The ability to engage in a digital world and perform tasks effectively is highly dependent on
digital skills functioning together within a wider framework of soft skills such as critical and
innovative thinking, complex problem solving, communication, and ability to collaborate."
(Lyons et al., 2019, p. 2)
The report comments on the importance of the "soft skills" in successfully applying technical
know-how to solve real problems as they enhance an individual’s ability to put digital technolo-
gies effectively into practice. The authors even go as far as aiming to redefine the process of
acquiring digital skills - moving beyond the current narrow focus on basic technical abilities
to include a more complete set of socio-technical skills. They believe that it is no longer solely
about achieving gains in technical access, knowledge and expertise,
"but also the ability to apply that knowledge so as to reach tangible and meaningful outcomes in
the workplace and beyond" (Lyons et al., 2019, p. 4).
Machine learning is utilized to create a predictive model for relieving the first-time daily
managers of hybrid work from one of the challenges expressed in the interviews.
1.1
Purpose statement
The purpose of this thesis is to: (1) Outline the challenges of managing a hybrid team from a first-
time daily manager perspective. (2) Investigate whether the type of hybrid work model, which
is implemented in an organization, can explain the experiences that first-time daily managers
have when managing hybrid teams. (3) evaluate whether a predictive machine learning based on
data collected by Epinion can relieve first-time daily managers from an identified key challenge,
which is experienced when managing hybrid teams.
To investigate first-time daily managers’ experiences in managing a hybrid team, as well as
whether this can be explained by the type of hybrid work model implemented in their organization,
Page 3 of 238
==PAGE:13==
1
INTRODUCTION
Master Thesis
a qualitative approach is applied. Herein, interview-data from seven first-time daily managers
and six employees from five different Danish knowledge-based organizations is collected and
analyzed. Subsequently, a predictive model is modeled based on data collected by Epinion from
Danish employees with the aim of relieving first-time daily managers from negative experiences
when managing hybrid teams. The model will look at employee patterns to predict their desired
number of days to WFH, thus supporting managers in scheduling within a hybrid work model.
1.1.1
Research question
What key challenges do first-time daily managers in knowledge-based organizations experience
when managing a team within a hybrid work model? And can human resource officers in
knowledge-based organizations use a predictive model based on the data from Epinion to prevent
one of the identified key challenges?
1. What is currently known from literature about the challenges that managers in general
face when managing hybrid teams?
2. Which challenges does a hybrid work model bring for first-time daily managers working
within knowledge-based organizations? And can the type of hybrid work model explain
which challenges first-time daily managers experience when managing a hybrid team?
3. Which of the identified key challenges makes sense for knowledge-based organizations to
solve? And can this challenge be solved using a predictive model based on the data from
Epinion?

are assigned identifiers to differentiate between them. These are stated in Table 3 together with
additional relevant interviewee information.

2
Literature Review
Throughout this section, literature on subjects relevant for the project are presented. This is to
give the reader a basic understanding of the essential concepts necessary for the thesis work.
The literature section is divided into two separate part. In the first part, concepts related to
management and a hybrid work model (HWM) within an organization are presented. In the
second part, concepts related to machine learning and how to use this method for predictive
modeling is presented.
2.1
The first-time daily manager
In Essentials of Contemporary Management, Jones and George (2015) define management as
"the planning, organizing, leading and controlling of human and other resources to achieve
organizational goals efficiently and effectively" (Jones & George, 2015, p. 5)
A manager’s success in performing the four tasks mentioned above; planning, organizing, leading
and controlling (defined as the four tasks of management) are essential for the work of managers
at all levels (Jones & George, 2015, p. 8). Jones and George (2015) establish the three hierarchical
levels of management: first-line managers (also called daily managers) middle managers and
top managers. This thesis will only concern the daily managers, who are responsible for daily
supervision of employees (Jones & George, 2015, p. 13).
Even though managers at all three levels work with the four tasks of management, they do so
in different ways and to varying extend. Traditionally, the tasks of planning, organizing and
controlling are more pronounced the higher up managers come in the hierarchy, whereas the
task of leading weighs heaviest for managers lower down in the hierarchy - a pattern which is
illustrated in Figure 1 (Jones & George, 2015, p. 15)
Figure 1: The four functions of management for top-, middle- and daily managers.
Page 6 of 238
==PAGE:16==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
A first-time daily manager (FTDM) is created when a professional who has no prior experience
of leading a team is chosen based on prior performance in their assigned jobs (Raulgaonkar,
2021, 3).
More than 10 years ago, Hales (2007) described characteristics of the daily manager (DM), their
role and responsibilities, and recently Raulgaonkar (2021) did the same, also underlining the role
and challenges that arise when a DM is appointed for the first time. In the following, descriptions
of the DM in general and FTDM from the two authors are presented. The frame will be set for
what a FTDM is and what challenges lie in this role.
Hales (2007) commented on the way the DM’s role shifted from one of routine supervision,
primarily focused on routine planning, scheduling and monitoring of work, to a wider manage-
ment responsibility for business performance. Raulgaonkar (2021) reaches the same conclusion
and states that even though the core role of the DM is still the supervision of their team, radical
organizational changes have introduced a number of other responsibilities to the DM, includ-
ing enacting organizational policies, implementing strategies, coaching each individual team
member and providing organizational leadership to name a few. Consequently, inherent in the
role, is its position in the intersection between broad strategic intent and specific operational
implementation:
"The FLM role is the point at which organizational strategy and policy become real in that
abstract managerial intentions and aspirations are translated into operational realities in the
form of concrete actions on the part of employees" (Hales, 2007, p. 166)
The role is filled with contradictions and competing expectations from above and below, involv-
ing dissonance between accountability and lack of authority or involvement in decision-making.
This leaves DMs "caught in the crossfire" (Raulgaonkar, 2021, p. 6) between their mid-level
managers and team members, leading them into a position of doubt when it comes to their tasks.
In the end results in improvisations on how to manage their teams (Hales, 2007, p. 163-164). In
fact, "52% of managers mentioned that they use their own previous experience in making their
managerial decisions, while 26% said they rely on their intuition, and 22% use decision making
techniques" (Raulgaonkar, 2021, p. 5). The improvisations made due to inexperience may in
the end compromise the strategies made on a higher level of the organizations, thus potentially
degrading the value creation within it (Hales, 2007, p. 152-154,164-166).
According to both Hales (2007) and Raulgaonkar (2021), many DMs are ill-equipped to take
on the role of manager, only having a background as specialists in their field. The skills of
an individual performer differ greatly from the skills needed as a manager who is responsible
for the performance of a team. Because of this, some first-time daily managers (FTDMs) drift
into the role of being the senior professional in a team rather than transitioning into their role
Page 7 of 238
==PAGE:17==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
of management and supervision. To accommodate this lack of readiness that many FTDMs
experience, organizations need to focus on training them and teach them how to tackle this role.
Unfortunately,
"organizations do not invest adequate training and development in the role. In 30 per cent of
organizations, FLMs receive no management training, particularly formal management training
leading to a recognized award, either prior to taking up their position or, indeed, subsequently"
(Hales, 2007, p. 166).
Although DMs are the largest group of managers in any organization, and arguably the most
important one, it is the most neglected in terms of training and development. The attitudes from
the senior management are cause of this neglect, when they do not prioritize the resources to
make sure that FTDM are equipped to take on their new role. This neglect is what pushes the
FTDM to engage in the improvisations mentioned previously, resulting in lowering value for the
organization (Raulgaonkar, 2021, p. 7).
Some organizations do assist their FTDM transition into the management role trough training
programs, which is the defining factor in FTDMs’ success.
2.2
The hybrid work model
"Working from home" (WFH), "teleworking", "telecommuting", "remote work" - it goes by
many names. Different ways of working away from the office has been a subject of interest
since the 1970s where the term telecommuting was denoted as working away from the office,
primarily by using telecommunication as a substitute for traveling to work (Nordqvist, 1978).
Many authors of the latest literature on the subject of remote work use the definition of telework
made by The European Framework Agreement on Telework:
“Telework is a form of organizing and/or performing work, using information technology, in the
context of an employment contract/relationship, where work, which could also be performed at
the employer’s premises, is carried out away from those premises on a regular basis" Stoian,
Caraiani, Anica-Popa, Dascalu, and Lungu (2022).
The incitement and structuring of WFH has been evolving over the years and has become
highly normalized during COVID-19. Gratton (2022) emphasizes that before the pandemic,
organizations were experiencing democratic and social changes that would influence the working
culture. The traditional three-stage career path; full-time education, -job and -pension was
fading out as employees would value family, traveling, starting their own company etc. and thus
creating a multi level work life (Gratton, 2022). According to Gratton (2022) organizations have
previously been reluctant to initiate major changes:
Page 8 of 238
==PAGE:18==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
"... for many companies, their experience with flexible place and time working were small scale,
and leaders and executives resisted change on a larger scale" (Gratton, 2022)
During the pandemic, leaders and employees were forced to rethink their perception of work-life
in terms of time and location and to overcome their previous reluctance about remote work
(Gratton, 2022; Grzegorczyk, Mariniello, Nurski, & Schraepen, 2021; Konovalova, Petrenko,
& Aghgashyan, 2022). WFH had become so frequent and widespread that both employers and
employees were showing a preference for "a higher share of teleworking hours compared to
pre-pandemic levels" (Grzegorczyk et al., 2021). Even though workers have expressed a desire
to telework more than before the pandemic, permanent telework is not preferable. A study of job
quality and mental health conducted during COVID-19-related telework supports this. The study
concludes that "telework improves job quality mostly when it takes place as a part-time work
arrangement" (Grzegorczyk et al., 2021, p. 11).
In the wake of the pandemic, many knowledge-based organizations (KBOs) began to implement
a hybrid work model (HWM), which entails a mix of on-site and remote work. Finding an
optimal HWM can be difficult and is being discussed by many these years (Chafi, Hultberg, &
Yams, 2022). In the following, different frameworks for identifying types of HWM is introduced.
Moreover, characteristics of successfully implementing a HWM are presented and attitudes
towards these from different levels of organizations are presented.
2.2.1
Types of hybrid work models
How hybrid work should be structured and what models are considered to be most suitable,
depend on the organization and the type of work done within the organization (Todd, 2022).
Sometimes the personal needs or preferences of workers and management within an organization
will also affect the choice of HWM (Grzegorczyk et al., 2021, p. 2).
In recent years, literature has tried to identify different HWMs to ensure the best working
environment. Griffis (2021) identifies three common hybrid work models: office-first (OF),
office-occasional (OO) and remote-first (RF). The office-first model considers a work model
where most of the work is done in the office. It is allowed to work remotely if necessary, but it
is not the standard. Leadership will take place from the office Griffis (2021). Office-occasional
encourages employees to come into the office a few days a week - either of their choosing or
specific days planned by management Griffis (2021). In the remote-first model, employees have
the opportunity to come into the office but are not required to do so. The office is more a resource
for the employees, a place they can have meetings, connect with colleagues or work if it is their
preferred workspace Griffis (2021).
Page 9 of 238
==PAGE:19==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
There is no correct or optimal HWB that any organization can implement and get the most out
of. However, there are essential aspects that an organization must do/take into account before
deciding on what type of HWM to implement. According to Konovalova et al. (2022),
"the effectiveness of the hybrid model is determined by a well thought-out program of actions
taken by the company to organize work, investments in digital infrastructure and support for
virtual cooperation" (Konovalova et al., 2022, p. 552).
Accordingly, both Ofluoglu et al. (2021) and Konovalova et al. (2022) also enhance the im-
portance of having a solid digital infrastructure, including: digital platforms for collaboration,
the necessary IT solutions and well-functioning internet access at home. Moreover, the authors
emphasize the necessity for defining standards, guidelines and expectations for a HWM to avoid
losing control and guarantee that the system runs smoothly. These should cover aspects such as:
drawing up procedures and etiquette for conducting online as well as in-person meetings, main-
taining corporate culture, managing employee well-being, optimizing communication channels,
making working schedules and more (Konovalova et al., 2022; Ofluoglu et al., 2021). Moreover,
more than 60% of managers consider it necessary to increase spending on virtual collaboration
tools, compared to what it is today (Konovalova et al., 2022, p. 554).
2.2.2
Advantages and challenges of hybrid work
Having established what hybrid work is, it is now relevant to investigate its advantages and
challenges. It is a well discussed topic, and previous studies identify central issues and gains in
relation to this (Bailey & Kurland, 2002; Kurkland & Bailey, 1999). Early on, Kurkland and
Bailey (1999) categorized advantages and challenges of hybrid work in three categories: organi-
zational, individual and societal. Moving on, the focus will be on individual and organizational
advantages/challenges to explore how a HWM affects employees/managers (individual) and
organizations as a whole (organizational).
Recent research conducted by Dansk Erhverv comments on one of the major incitement of imple-
menting hybrid work: the productivity of employees (Nielsen & B. Olsson, 2021). According to
the survey, asking organizations and employees of varying fields of activity, the organizations and
the employees are disagreeing concerning productivity when WFH (Nielsen & B. Olsson, 2021).
Most organizations (42%) are experiencing the same productivity among their employees when
WFH, while 25% of the organizations believe their employees are more productive when WFH
and 32% believe they are less productive when WFH. The surveyed employees have answered
before and after COVID-19 how they experience their productivity. Most of the employees (50%
before and 51% after) believe they are more productive at home, 36% and 42% answered the
Page 10 of 238
==PAGE:20==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
productivity is unaffected and only 13% and 8% are less productive when WFH (Nielsen &
B. Olsson, 2021).
"While the majority of the employees consider their productivity highest when working from
home, organizations sees it differently; there are more organizations that believe their
employees are less productive working from home than there are organizations that experience
higher productivity working from home" - (Nielsen & B. Olsson, 2021)
Recent literature on the effects of a HWM on important organizational and individual factors
shows a picture of broad implications. Authors report a wide range of factors, divided into
advantages and challenges, when implementing a HWM. The ones reported most and by multiple
authors in the literature found through the study made in this thesis, are displayed in Table 2. This
table shows organizational advantages and challenges followed by individual advantages
and challenges, with reference to the articles that have found these to be present in their research.
The factors which are most frequently reported in literature are displayed at the top. Subsequently,
the factors are displayed with descending frequency.
There have been reported other factors than the ones displayed in the table. These have not been
included due to only one article pointing them out, thus not showing enough backing for them as
prominent factors affected by a HWM.

From Table 2, patters emerge, which are interesting to further comment on. Many authors state
that a greater productivity is observed for employees when WFH due to i.e. the gained flexibility
and WLB provided by telework, and an increased freedom to concentrate without office noise and
division, which makes teleworkers feel better able to perform their jobs (Beno, 2018; Ofluoglu
et al., 2021; Stoian et al., 2022). However, in contrast to this, some authors observe a lower
productivity among employees when WFH due to the feeling of isolation, which can create
distinct challenges that negatively affect work productivity (Stoian et al., 2022). A number of
authors report a mistrust from managers in employees when it comes to productivity, because it is
believed that managers cannot manage what they cannot see (Beno, 2018; Harrington & Ruppel,
1999). Nonetheless, the results are highly skewed towards experiencing a higher productivity
among employees, when looking at the amount of studies that have concluded this.
In regard to managers trusting their employees, and employees trusting their managers, some
authors report a higher trust within a HWM due to the increased autonomy felt by employees
(Ofluoglu et al., 2021, p. 140). Others establish that hybrid arrangements create less inherent
trust than exists in an in-house relationship (Beno, 2022).
Another factor which is displayed as both an advantage and a challenge is the fact that when
WFH, many workers and managers experience working longer hours. This is in some ways good
for the productivity of the company, but can interfere with workers’ personal lives and result in
higher work loads and sometimes cause stress (Grzegorczyk et al., 2021; Stoian et al., 2022).
The two most frequently reported individual challenges when implementing a HWM are so-
cial isolation and lack of social interaction. Inherently, these sound similar and maybe even
Page 13 of 238
==PAGE:23==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
interchangeable. The social isolation that some experience is usually connected to prolonged
absence of physical engagement with the workplace, which isolates the person from the rest of
the team (Beno, 2018). This is when workers begin to feel disconnected with their team and lose
professional relationships (Ofluoglu et al., 2021, p. 141). Feelings of social isolation will often
negatively affect work productivity (Stoian et al., 2022, p. 20). In "lack of social interaction" lies
more the loss of the small everyday interactions that come naturally when working in an office.
This can also negatively affect the sense of belonging to the office and coworkers but without a
sense of isolation from the work place. As Beno (2022) puts it:
"on-site, a sense of camaraderie may be achieved in daily communication, but a hybrid team
lacks the daily interaction around the common water cooler" - (Beno, 2022) 37
An extended lack of social interactions may lead to a feeling of social isolation.
Social interaction plays a role in more than creating a feeling of belonging and connectivity.
When workers are regularly brought together in a common space, mangers are more able to en-
sure that team-building, knowledge exchange and corporate cultures do not suffer (Grzegorczyk
et al., 2021, p. 11). This leads to the most frequent organizational challenge reported in recent
literature - the managerial challenges connected to the HWM.
Literature often reports a struggling management, and that these struggles may not be fully cov-
ered by the factors listed in the table. Beno (2022) establishes that generally, "direct supervision
over work is very problematic" when managing a hybrid team. Struggling with team management
can lead to problems with performance management, team coordination and building trust, which
are essential aspects for successfully managing a team. Management challenges can convey a
desire to strengthen control over the activities of employees WFH, leading to micromanagement
mechanisms (Konovalova et al., 2022, p. 550).
An aspect, which could affect the types of advantage and challenges which are found in literature
is the type of HWM the organizations have implemented. However, this is not reported in the
articles, which makes it a relevant aspect to further investigate.
A pattern, which also emerges, is that there are more organizational challenges than individual
and that there are more individual advantages than there are organizational. This indicates a high
that organizations are struggling more to adapt to the HWM than individuals and therefore pose
a relevance to focus on the organizational challenges.
Going further, the managerial challenges from the direct manager’s point of view will be the
point of focus, to present what is currently established as being key challenges for managing a
team in a HWM.
Page 14 of 238
==PAGE:24==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
2.3
The hybrid manager
The previous sections concern (1) classical management theory and (2) the affects hybrid work
has on organizations and its members. Now it is time to connect the two and look into what
effects hybrid work has on the managerial task according to literature.
According to Jones and George (2015)
"organizational change can bring alterations in the ways managers carry out the critical tasks
of planning, organizing, leading, and controlling and the ways they perform their managerial
roles - (Jones & George, 2015, p. 283),
and it is safe to say that changing from the classical work model to a HWM is a fundamental
organizational change. The hybrid work model has brought with it a challenge to managers:
"do they trust their employees to work outside of their presence? A perceived loss of control may
be experienced by a manager as employees disappear from the manager’s daily gaze" -
(Harrington & Ruppel, 1999),
According to Beno (2018) "the key to success in telework is an effective leader", and on the
same track Stoian et al. (2022) state that "leadership practices are vital to ensure the successful
implementation of a telework model". The authors advocate for a further investigation of the
HWM, as this new work model directly influences the responsibilities of organizational leaders
i.e. managers (Stoian et al., 2022, p. 20). Chen and Sriphon (2021) conclude that "good managers
need excellent leadership skills", and Stoian et al. (2022) state that "leadership practices are
vital to ensure the successful implementation of a telework model".
Beno (2018) divide the main team-leadership skills into what they entail for managers of
traditional teams and managers of virtual teams:
"Traditional leadership skills: Developing and articulating the team’s mission and objectives,
ensuring clean lines of communication, updating the team regularly on progress, measuring
performance [and] mentoring team members" - (Beno, 2018).
"Virtual leadership skills: Building trust in the team, precision in communication, technological
know-how/aptitude [and] traditional team leadership skills" - (Beno, 2018)
From this it is clear that managing people virtually requires the same leadership skills as it does
managing people in the traditional sense, but it also requires additional aspects, as these become
crucial when the physical presence is absent.
Some criteria that are highlighted as beneficial for managers of hybrid teams are: "willingness,
trust in employees, result-oriented management, communication, flexibility, work experience,
Page 15 of 238
==PAGE:25==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
(...) good motivational capabilities", support and ability to give feedback (Beno, 2018, 2022).
Out of these, managers agree that building trust and creating the right values and behaviors are
the main elements which influence leadership in teleworking and therefore the most important
managerial tasks at a distance (Beno, 2018, 2022; Harrington & Ruppel, 1999). Harrington and
Ruppel (1999) further states that a HWM even necessitates a greater degree of trust between the
team and their manager.
Trust is an essential mediator of multiple aspects within organizational effectiveness. It is an
initial step to building relationships and essential to manage these effectively, a factor for success
in collaboration and cooperation between people, an important factor that mediates leadership
effectiveness and is highly significant to team performance (Chen & Sriphon, 2021). In this
way, trust is tightly connected to the social factors that were shown in Table 2 to be challenging
for many individuals working within a HWM. Social isolation and lack of everyday social
interactions are difficulties that have also been put on the tables of DMs. As DMs are respon-
sible for the well-being and productivity of their employees, and both of these are affected by
negative impacts of missing social interactions due to a HWM, it is up to the DM of a team to
accommodate the social challenges that arise. Dealing with the social challenges that come with
a HWM puts another layer onto the increased responsibilities of the DM.
A culture of trust is able to develop when "leaders show trustworthiness in terms of reliability,
openness, ability, and benevolence, and fulfill institutional trust building mechanisms" (Chen &
Sriphon, 2021). This puts the responsibility of building this trust on management, and especially
the management near to the employees - namely the daily management. When daily managers
are left with the responsibility of creating trust with their employees and supporting their team
socially to enhance success of a HWM, it requires experience and knowledge of how to build this
trust. As it was established in section 2.1, many FTDM are not very well equipped to carry out
their responsibilities of their new role as managers, due to lack of training and preparation for the
role. Moreover, FTDMs’ novelty to the role will affect their ability to build the necessary trust
with their employees, since previously mentioned factors for creating trust (reliability, openness,
ability etc.) need time to be established. All this makes it especially important for DMs to build
trust with their employees but especially difficult for the FTDM to do so.
In addition to trust, organizations need to establish clear definitions of objectives, roles and
responsibilities, in order to make a HWM successful (Beno, 2022). These definitions may be
drawn up by higher layers of organizations, but they must be upheld and communicated by the
DMs close to the employees. Therefore, if such definitions are not put in place or are not clear
enough, this could cause complications for the DMs.
Working in teams require coordination, and as the need for coordination increases, as it does in a
HWM, so does the requirements for communication (Jacobsen & Thorsvik, 2013, p. 249). This
Page 16 of 238
==PAGE:26==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
puts even more pressure on the abilities of the FTDM, as communication requires additional
skills and effort (Konovalova et al., 2022, p. 522). I a HWM
"While asynchronous communication increases, synchronous communication decreases, which
makes it harder for workers to exchange more complex information, converge on the meaning of
information, and build relationships" - (Chafi et al., 2022, p. 2)
The communication between the employees and their managers "is a critical factor in the
effective functioning of virtual teams" (Beno, 2018). Use of communication can assist managers
mentoring employees, so they do not fall into the trap of social isolation, that a HWM can cause
(Beno, 2018). Communication skills also help mangers to maximize trust and minimize stress
(Chen & Sriphon, 2021).
Throughout this section, it is evident, that the expansion of the use of hybrid work implies a more
complex organization of work and will require a change in cooperate culture (in relation to trust)
and engagement management tools (in relation to socialization and communication), which in
most cases need to be changed by decision makers: managers and executives (Konovalova et al.,
2022). Much of the literature used in section 2.2.2 and 2.3 underline the challenges of hybrid
work and how these affect managers. However, the literature do not specify what management
level they refer to when remarking on the effects that a HWM has in managers and their job-tasks.
If an organization wishes to get at the challenges that FTDMs face when managing a hybrid
team, it is important to make a clear distinction between the different layers of management.
In this section, some comments have been made on how aspects such as trust building, communi-
cation skills and forming of social bonds have possibly become responsibilities of the DM within
the HWM. However, it is not something which is outlined or specifically investigated in previous
literature. The effects of being new to management and therefore not being trained in the tools
and approaches that are required of DMs, have also not been investigated in a hybrid work setting
before. It is therefore interesting to further explore specifically how FTDM experience their role
as managers of hybrid teams. This is to examine if findings in this thesis will be consistent with
what is reported in literature for mangers in general or if there are specific challenges for FTDM
that organizations need to be aware of.
2.4
Knowledge-based organizations
According to Zack (2003), When defining what characterizes a knowledge-based organization
(KBO):
"The degree to which knowledge is an integral part of a company is defined not by what the
company sells but by what it does and how it is organized" - (Zack, 2003).
Page 17 of 238
==PAGE:27==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
This means that it is the intangible assets of an organization that create the value (Zack, 2003).
KBOs must consider multiple aspects of working with knowledge to create value and achieve
competitive advantage (Zack, 2003). Herein, knowledge sharing and learning from the past
are fundamental, as these are the cornerstones for alignment of workers and a functioning
organization.
A survey conducted by Panopto (2018) investigates the costs of inadequate knowledge sharing.
The survey finds that turnover rate and access to knowledge are related to each other and em-
ployees spend on average 5.3 hours per week waiting for assistance or insights from coworkers.
In this time, the employees who need help will either be delayed or recreate their colleagues’
knowledge, using extra time and duplicating work. In Section 2.2.2, knowledge sharing was
identified as the most frequently mentioned organizational challenge when employees have the
possibility of working hybrid. Therefore, when an organization chooses to implement a HWM,
they need to reconsider how to maintain knowledge, to avoid isolating the knowledge in the
organization.
According to (Nielsen & B. Olsson, 2021, fig. 2) it is primarily knowledge-driven fields that are
experiencing a conversion towards a HWM. Additionally, the McKinsey Global Institute made a
study of more than 2000 types of activities in more than 800 professions, where they concluded
that highly qualified and highly educated employees have the highest potential for converting to
and expanding the sale of hybrid and remote work. They found that
"20% of employees in such activities as collecting and processing information, communicating
with other people, training and consulting, as well as data coding can work remotely from three
to five days a week as efficiently as in the office" - (Konovalova et al., 2022, p. 551).
Because of this, it is the KBOs that will most heavily experience the challenges connected to
implementing a HWM presented in section 2.2.2.
2.5
Human resources management in a knowledge-based organizations
Having established what characterizes a KBO, it is now relevant to identify where in the
organization changes can be initiated to effectively utilize and apply knowledge. As Wi´sniewska
and Wi´sniewski (2012) describes:
... human resource management is the process of managing human talents to achieve
organization’s objectives - (Wi´sniewska & Wi´sniewski, 2012)
Human resources management (HRM) departments play a crucial role in knowledge management,
thus HRM has a significant impact on the effectiveness of a KBO (Wi´sniewska & Wi´sniewski,
2012). The HRM department within an organization has the ability to see the organization as a
Page 18 of 238
==PAGE:28==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
whole, and is therefore equipped to implement activities in all departments in the organization
and not just locally. Wi´sniewska and Wi´sniewski (2012) presents key tasks of the HR department
in KBOs, and some of these that are highly relevant for this thesis are:
"Adaptation of working conditions to the needs of talents and knowledge workers, (...)
supporting the use of modern solutions for the rapid acquisition of knowledge, acquiring new
skills and continuous accumulation of knowledge in the department of human resources and
effective management of the pooled resources of knowledge" - (Wi´sniewska & Wi´sniewski,
2012).
These three tasks involve HR departments’ ability to support organizations and the people
working within them. HR has the ability to allocate resources within all departments in an
organization, which means that it is the HR departments that must take responsibility for
relieving organizational challenges (Wi´sniewska & Wi´sniewski, 2012). This includes challenges
such as the ones experienced by FTDMs when managing hybrid teams. The actions which HR
departments have the ability to take concerning optimizing the conditions of working hybrid are
also emphasized by Pillai and Prasad (2023). In this research, it is suggested that HR departments
should foster the positive experiences when working hybrid by "reorienting effective training
among employees", to utilized the productivity potential among workers (Pillai & Prasad, 2023).
2.6
Machine learning as a tool for managing
Machine learning is a category of artificial intelligence where computers are able to learn based
on experience from data. The computer analyzes and interprets patterns and structures in data to
modify and improve actions for prediction (Alzubi, Nayyar, & Kumar, 2018).
Today, machine learning is broadly used across various fields, including different areas of
management. Herein, there are examples of it being used to adapt overall marketing behaviors,
improve business operations in terms of production, procurement, costumer service and quality
assurance, gain greater insight in consumer needs and trends, etc.. In recent years, more new
research has been published in regards to using machine learning to predict aspects related to the
people in organizations, covering aspects such as employee retention, stress and scheduling of
employees etc.(Jain et al., 2020; Marvin et al., 2021; Reddy et al., 2018).
This research signifies that machine learning is capable of ....
2.7
Machine learning in this thesis
Having established that machine learning is a legitimate tool to use in management when solving
certain types of problems, the method is explained in further detail. This is done so a quantitative
analysis can be made on survey-data collected by Epinion with the aim of solving one or more
of the challenges found in the qualitative analysis.
Page 19 of 238
==PAGE:29==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
In order to clarify which machine learning techniques and evaluation metrics to use in the thesis,
it is important to consider if a Supervised or Unsupervised Learning approach should be used. In
this case, a Supervised Learning approach is used since labeled data is used, which means the
outcome of each observation is already known as the dependent variable. Since the dependent
variable is a multi-class categorical variable1, predicting this variable is a classification problem.
Knowing the type of problem makes it possible to choose which machine learning techniques to
use. The chosen machine learning techniques are Random Forest Classification and Naive Bayes
Classifier which will be explained in the following sections. These algorithms have been chosen
as they are some of the highest performing algorithms in classification problems, according to
previous work (Akkaya & Çolako˘glu, 2019; Brownlee, 2020; Pirge, 2020). Further explanation
of why these algorithms have been chosen, is presented in the methodology in Section 3.9
(modelleing).
Random Forest Classifier
The Random Forest Classifier (RFC) algorithm is based upon the Decision Tree Classifier
(DTC) algorithm (Zhou, 2021). In Figure 2 a simple example of a Decision Tree is visualized.
The purpose of this decision tree is to separate the top string into two classes, 0 and 1, by using
the features ’Blue’ and ’Underlined’. As all the red numbers are one, the first split is done by
color. Since the ’No’ branch2 only consists of red ones, this branch is now closed. However, the
’Yes’ branch still consists of zeros and ones, therefore the other feature ’Underlined’ is used,
splitting the binary numbers. All the features are now used, and all the branches are now closed.
The final tree did not split the numbers perfectly, but as well as it could with the given features.
The branch with both zeros and one will predict zero, as the majority of numbers are zeros. This
means, if the observation is both blue and underlined, it will be predicted to be zero even though
it can contain one.
Figure 2: Decision Tree classification example.
1A multi-class categorical variable is a variable containing more than two classes
2A branch is a subsection of the decision tree, which is denoted by the splits. When a tree splits it will have two
or more branches.
Page 20 of 238
==PAGE:30==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
The Random Forest Classifier uses the Decision Tree approach by applying a large number of
individual decision trees, which operate as an ensemble3. Each tree in the classifier predicts
a class, then the classifier evaluates which class is predicted the most, and then categorize
this observation to belong to the specific class. In Figure 3, a simple example of a Random
Forest Classifier is displayed. In this example, six Decision Trees are generated, predicting the
observation to be either 0 or 1. As there are four trees predicting 1 and only three trees predicting
0, the observation is predicted to belong to class 1.
Figure 3: Random Forest Classification example
The Random Forest Classifier is simple, yet a strong tool as the algorithm is far more robust
than any of the individual decision trees, as it estimates the average prediction on relatively
uncorrelated predictions of trees. The algorithm ensures that the trees in the model are not too
correlated by using the following two methods: Bagging or Bootstrap Aggregation and Random
feature selection. Using these methods to ensure tree diversity, the trees will be protected by
each individual error and hence build a more robust learning algorithm (Yiu, 2019).
Naive Bayes Classifier
The Naive Bayes Classifier is based on Bayes’ Theorem and consist of multiple learning
algorithms that share the same principle: All features are independent of each other. The Bayes’
Theorem used in the Naive Bayes Classifier estimates the probability that an event occurs given
another already occurred event’s probability. This probability is expressed as:
P(y|x1,...,xn) = P(x1|y)P(x2|y)...P(xn|y)P(y)
P(x1)P(x2)...P(xn)
(1)
In the equation, y represent the variable to be classified, i.e. the probability of an event occuring
based on event X. X represents the n independent variables used in the model, i.e. the events
that has already occurred. The equation can be simplified as the denominator does not change
for all entries in data and it is a multi-class classification problem. Since the model that will be
3Ensemble methods is characterized by using multiple learning algorithm to obtain a better prediction than a
learning algorithm could do alone

probability. Applying these simplifications to the equation, the Naive Bayes Classifier uses
following expression:

Evaluation metrics
When different machine learning models have been implemented and these have made their
predictions, there is a need for common evaluation metrics of their performance. This will
include metrics calculating the Accuracy, F1 Score and Confusion Matrix. these metrics all work
well for multi-class classification problems. Each of these metrics contributes in their own way
to a greater understanding of how well the models perform. (Mishra, 2018).
Accuracy
The accuracy is a simple evaluation metric, which calculates the percentage of correctly classified
observations. Mathematically, it is expressed as:
Accuracy = Correctly classified obs.
Total number of obs.
(3)
The advantages of this evaluation metric is that it is easy to implement and interpret and when
data is balanced, accuracy is a good estimate of how well the model performs. On the other hand,
if the data is imbalanced, accuracy will have a tendency of predicting the larger class, creating
bias towards this class.
An example is having a model that predicts whether an email is spam or not. If 90% of the
target variable4 are normal emails and only 10% of the emails are spam and the model predicts
all observations as normal emails, the model will achieve an accuracy of 0.90 (90%). This is
problematic, as the high accuracy indicates the model is good at predicting spam or not spam,
even though no emails were classified as spam. For this reason, it is important to investigate the
distribution of the target variable before modeling, when using accuracy as an evaluation metric.
4A target variable is the variable whose values are to be predicted or modeled by other variables.
Page 22 of 238
==PAGE:32==
2
LITERATURE REVIEW
Master Thesis
Confusion Matrix, Recall and Precision
When a Confusion Matrix i generated, it is possible to evaluate how a model has distributed the
classifications. In Figure 4, an example of a binary-class confusion matrix is illustrated. The
matrix consists of an actual class (the true values) and a predicted class (the predicted values).
The four boxes represent the number of predicted values versus the actual values. The first box
TP (true positives) is the true predicted values belonging to classification 1, whereas TN (true
negatives) is the true predicted belonging to classification 0. FP (false positive) is when the
model predicts the observation to be 1 when it is actually 0, and FN (false negative) is when the
model predicts the observation to be 0 when it is actually 1.
Figure 4: Confusion Matrix Example.
The information provided by the confusion matrix is often used when calculating Precision and
Recall, which are used to evaluate the performance of a model. Figure 4 shows what information
is used to calculate precision and recall. Mathematically, Recall and Precision are expressed as:
Recall =
TP
TP+FN
Precision =
TP
TP+FP
The Recall value represents the number of observations that are predicted correctly from all the
positive classes. The Precision value represents the number of observations that are positive
from all the classes that have been predicted as positive. Ideally, both values should be as high as
possible, hence FN and FP should be as low as possible.
F1 Score
The F1 score is the harmonic mean of Precision and Recall from the confusion matrix and is
preferred when dealing with an imbalanced class distribution as it is sensitive to data distribution.
The metric can be used both in single-class evaluation and in multi-class evaluation. In multi-
class evaluation, the F1-score rates how well a model classifies the different classes, meaning it
is not an overall score but a score for the success of each separate class. The greater the F1 score,
the better the performance of the model. Mathematically, it is expressed as:
F1 = 2·
1
1
precision +
1
recall
(4)

3
Methodology
This section presents the methodology used in the thesis work. Herein, the purpose, approach
and development of research design are described to give the reader a clear understanding of the
methodological choices made before the study. It is described how the mixed methods approach
was applied as well as how and why the two methods are connected. Subsequently, a short
description of the process of the study illustrates the flow of the work and the steps taken to arrive
at the conclusion. Hereafter, the collection of primary data (interviews) and the qualitative data
analysis method are outlined. Then the collection of secondary data (survey data from Epinion)
and the quantitative data analysis method are outlined. Finally, it is described how the literature
search was conducted in the making of the literature review.
The overall choice of philosophy, approach, research design and analysis procedures for a
research project is illustrated using the research onion in Figure 5.
Figure 5: The "Research Onion"
The outer two ’layers’ consist of the research philosophy and approach, which are chosen by
the authors to fit with the overall purpose and research question for the project. They lay the
ground for the assumptions and worldview undertaken in the development of knowledge in the
thesis (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 107-108). The layers that make out the research design are the
three layers marked with the darker shade of purple in the figure, namely: research strategy,
choice of method and time horizon. The research design is a general plan of how to answer the
Page 24 of 238
==PAGE:34==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
research question, which will contain clear objectives and specify data collection sources. The
research design is rooted in the research question and should be consistent with the research
philosophy and purpose of the research, thus providing valid justification for the specific choices
made to the design (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 136-137). Finally, the center of the onion entails
the techniques and procedures (often called the research tactics) chosen to collect the data and
conduct the analysis in this thesis. In the following, the purpose and philosophy (section 3.1),
approach to theory development (section 3.2) and research design (section 3.3) for the thesis are
presented. In addition to presenting the practical approach taken by the authors in these sections,
the relevant concepts of each layer are also described therein. The techniques and procedures for
data collection and analysis will be given in further detail in section 3.5, 3.6 and 3.9.
3.1
Purpose and philosophy
For classification of a research purpose, three types are often used in methods’ literature; ex-
ploratory, descriptive and explanatory. The purpose of this thesis, which is formulated in the
purpose statement and following research question, cannot be classified using only one of these
types. One part seeks to explore which challenges FTDMs experience when managing a hybrid
team, thus pursuing insights into a relatively new and unexplored area. Another part seeks to
try and explain why the challenges are experienced by looking into types of HWMs. The last
part wishes to predict a relationship between variables using machine learning (ML) models as a
means to resolve a managerial problem. Descriptive efforts were necessary as a foundation for
the predictive study, as this creates a clear picture of the situation portrayed by the investigated
data (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 139-140).
The research philosophy adopted in this thesis was the one of pragmatism. Thus, the formulation
of the research design was based on the purpose and research question for the project. Because
the research question has aspects of different purposes, a pragmatic philosophy was befitting.
This philosophy finds mixed methods appropriate as long as the most appropriate methods for
answering the research question are applied (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 119).
3.2
Approach to theory development
The second layer in the research onion deals with the approach to development of research. The
approach dictates the overall way in which the project is conducted. There are three fundamental
approaches to involve theory in a project; deduction (where data is developed from theory),
induction (where theory is developed from data) and abduction (a combination of deduction and
induction, where the researcher moves between the two). Thus, a deductive approach will seek to
describe what is happening whereas an inductive approach will seek to explain why something
Page 25 of 238
==PAGE:35==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
is happening and an abductive approach will be able to shed light on both aspects (Saunders et
al., 2007, p. 126).
The abductive approach was found most fitting for this thesis as it enables a combination of data
collection and analysis methods that have different strengths in connection to theory development.
First, existing theory was applied deductively to set the frame for collecting primary data. Then
the emerging themes and patterns from the analysis were used inductively to address parts of
the research question connected to exploring the situation and identify problem areas. Finally, a
solution proposal was approached deductively by creating a solution using machine learning.
This approach fell well in line with the pragmatic research philosophy, as it introduced multiple
ways of approaching a problem to reach valuable conclusions.
3.3
Research design
After formulating the adopted philosophy and approach for this thesis in the previous sections,
it is time to define a specific plan towards how the project will be carried out. This will entail
structure and constraints and be build on strategy, method and time horizon.
Research strategy
The first layer of the research design is the research strategy. Herein it is not so important to
identify what strategy is deployed but its ability to enable the researcher to answer the research
question. The strategy will therefore be rooted in the research question and the aim of the study,
as well as in the chosen philosophy underpinning the approach to research.
In this thesis, a cross case study was chosen to tackle the issues described in the problem
statement with elements of the research question being addressed using the survey strategy,
which is well in line with the pragmatism philosophy of this thesis. The case study strategy is
often used for inductive studies and survey strategy is often used for deductive studies.
The case study was chosen for the inductive part of the study as it is well suited for the ex-
ploratory aspects of the project, where little knowledge existed before undertaking the project
work (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 146). Herein, rich data was collected and used to compare across
different cases. The resources available for the thesis work also played a role in deciding to
do a case study. This strategy does not have a rigorous procedure to follow and therefore gave
more freedom to the authors when choosing data collection techniques and method of analysis.
Planning the thesis work as a case study made it possible to look at a certain type of managers
from different organizations and compare the gathered data about these. The aim was to gather
enough data to draw sound conclusions about which challenges this specific type of manager
experience when managing a hybrid team. For this purpose, the case study was considered a
well suited strategy. Moving on to the purpose of using predictive models to prevent negative
ramifications of managing a hybrid team, a different strategy had to be deployed, as the case
Page 26 of 238
==PAGE:36==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
study was not well suited for this aspect of the thesis. Here, the survey strategy was chosen.
The authors did not themselves collect the survey data for this part of the study. The survey
strategy was deployed to analyze the given data through descriptive statistics and following,
using machine learning models to make a prediction tool for managers to use.
Choice of method
The second layer of the research design is the choice of method. In this lies the choice be-
tween using qualitative and quantitative methods to collect and analyze data. It has become
increasingly popular in business and management research to use multiple methods to address
the research question when conducting a study. Within the multiple methods there are four
different possibilities of combining the methods, which each will help achieve different results
(Saunders et al., 2007, p. 151-152). A mixed method research was conducted to address the
research question. Mixed methods research "is a general term for when both quantitative and
qualitative data collection techniques and analysis procedures are used in a research design"
(Saunders et al., 2007, p. 152). In this, interview data is analyzed qualitatively, and the survey
data is also analyzed quantitatively, so these are not mixed.
A major advantage of using mixed methods was that the different methods were used for different
purposes in a study. The qualitative method was used to dive deep into specific people’s experi-
ences and attitudes towards managing hybrid teams, whereas the quantitative method was used
with a more macro perspective, where a predictive model was made based on the responses in a
survey. The mixed methods were applied in a sequential manner, where one was applied before
the other. First, the qualitative data collection was done, following an analysis of this. Thereafter,
the quantitative method was used to analyze existing survey data to see if one challenge the man-
agers experience, found in the qualitative analysis, could be solved using machine learning. Using
these in combination would give a perspective that none of them would be able to do on their own.
The choice of combining the qualitative and quantitative methods also roots itself in its practical
applicability for engineers in the modern day industry. As companies are becoming increasingly
digital, bridging the understanding between analytical business understanding and technical data
understanding is of great value. Oftentimes, people from the two worlds - business and data - do
not speak the same language, which can cause confusion or even conflict within organizations.
The aim of mixing these methods in the current study is to investigate how to make the two
come together and create a common understanding across the two languages. In organizations
that rely on both business and data, it is essential that these two are able to communicate in
order to reach a common goal. It is interesting to investigate how these methods can reach a
shared goal and examine the difficulties that follow a mix of these methods. As the authors
have both a technical background in the field of engineering as well as a background in areas of
business, management and entrepreneurship, they have deep interest in both aspects. Combining
Page 27 of 238
==PAGE:37==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
the technical mindset of engineering with the more analytical one of management is viewed by
the authors to present a relevant angle and to have a high potential to create interest for managers.
This notion is supported by Saunders et al. (2007, p. 127) with the statement: "In our experience,
most managers are familiar with deduction and much more likely to put faith in the conclusions
emanating from this approach". Therefore, by using deduction in the solution phase, there is
created an incentive for managers to adopt the results. This is, if they are in fact more willing to
trust the conclusions of a deductive approach.
Time horizon
The last layer of the research design entails the time horizon for the study. As for many academic
projects, this thesis was time constrained, which resulted in a cross sectional study of the
qualitative data. The quantitative data, which was not collected by the authors, was collected
over six periods in time, which gave it a longitudinal time horizon. Though the quantitative
data collection had been longitudinal, the analysis of the data was not such that the results were
compared over time. Thus, this aspect of the data gathering was not included into the models.
Having gone through the outer layers of the research onion and established the research design
for the thesis, it is time to concentrate on the center of the onion. This is related to the techniques
and procedures for data collection and data analysis. In the following section an overview of
the processes gone through to collect and analyze data is presented. Thereafter, the following
sections go into more detail of exactly how each step is done.
3.4
The thesis process
Before diving in to the details of the collection and methodology of data analysis, an overview
of the overall thesis process is presented in the following. This process entails the 13 steps
illustrated in Figure 6 described below.
Page 28 of 238
==PAGE:38==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
Figure 6: Thesis process
First, a research question was devised to set the over all problem formulation of the project. This
step is denoted phase 1. From this, the Project Refinement Cycle was entered, which consists of
5 phases, and is an iterative process. The cycle was gone through twice until a satisfying scope,
interview guide and data gathering was reached.
The first step in the Project Refinement Cycle, was phase 2, which in the cycle included
establishing an initial scope of the project through preliminary research exploration, to understand
the field of interest. This phase was closely connected with phase 3 in the first round of the cycle,
as this consisted of gathering relevant literature. The research exploration was conducted using
search strings and a loose search for articles to get an overview of what already existed on the
topics of the research question. This process is further explained in Section 3.10. Furthermore,
the overall choice of philosophy, approach and research design was established.
After both the project scope and relevant research of the topic were found, the first research
questions were established in phase 4.
Based on the research questions, a preliminary interview guide was designed in phase 5. Fol-
lowing this, phase 6 consisted of preliminary qualitative data gathering through semi-structured
interview with one interviewee relevant to the initial scope. Furthermore, this phase included
the initial understanding of the quantitative data, and how this could relate to the overall scope.
In this phase, it became clear that the scope needed to be narrowed and made more precise,
which was done in the second round of the cycle in phase 2. The second round of phase 3
and phase 4 found additional literature relevant for the revised scope, which was fundamental
for establishing appropriate research questions. This second research exploration was, like the
Page 29 of 238
==PAGE:39==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
one before, conducted using search strings and a loose search to understand new aspects of the
altered scope. In phase 5 of second round of the cycle, a revised interview guide was formulated
to fit with the revised scope of the project. In this phase more well-defined criteria were set up for
the interview-subjects of interest. Subsequently, interview data was gathered for the qualitative
study and the quantitative data was further explored in second round of phase 6. First by a more
thorough data understanding and then a descriptive analysis.
From this phase, the Project Refinement Cycle was exited, and phase 7 was initiated, which
involved selecting appropriate methods for both analyzing the qualitative and quantitative data.
From this phase, the process splits into two parts, one for the quantitative part and one for the
qualitative part. This is phase 8, which consists of coding and analyzing the qualitative data,
and phase 9, which consists of preparing the quantitative data for modeling. The reason why
phase 9 is influenced by phase 8, is because the findings in the qualitative analysis was used in
the quantitative analysis (also called proposed solution). Following the preparation of the data,
phase 10 could start, which involved modeling and evaluation of the quantitative data. Hereafter
in phase 11, the results from both the qualitative and quantitative parts were discussed, in terms
of the overall scope, methods used and the proposed solution. Lastly, in phase 12, the findings
from the analysis related to addressing the research question were documented in the report.
In the following sections, the techniques and procedures located at the center of the research
onion are presented. First, the collection method for the primary data is presented. Next, is the
qualitative analysis method for analyzing the primary data. Afterwards, it is described how the
secondary data was acquired and how this was analyzed using quantitative methods.
3.5
Data collection - primary data
Finishing off with the center of the research onion, the following section deals with first half of
this: data collection for the qualitative analysis. The primary data for the thesis was collected
from multiple sources relevant to the topic through semi-structured interviews and later used for
qualitative analysis. In the following it is described how the interview guide was formed, how
interviewees were approached and acquired and how the primary data was collected.
Interviews
Interviews are a commonly used method for qualitative data collection in research, as it can
gather valid and reliable data with a high degree of data-richness. Interviews can also assist in
formulating research questions and that way serve as empirical background for the investigation
of a subject (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 318). In this thesis, interviews are used both to formulate
the research question and then later to address this research question through investigating
relevant subjects. This thesis uses semi-structured interviews as this interview-type is well
suited for exploratory as well as explanatory studies due to its open yet structured approach to
Page 30 of 238
==PAGE:40==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
data-gathering(Saunders et al., 2007, p. 322).
Semi-structured interviews
Semi-structured interviews are a type of interviews, where the researcher will have a list of
themes and questions for conducting the interviews — however, the exact content and order
of questions for each interview may vary. Semi-structured interviews are well in line with the
abductive approach of the research strategy and will form a solid foundation for the analysis that
will be applied to find patterns from the gathered data.
In the semi-structured interviews of this thesis, a list of questions (also called an interview guide)
was completed based on a predetermined idea of what was wanted to get out of the interview.
The initial interview guide was made based on literature and a wish to explore the the research
topic openly. This guide was tested on an interviewee in the position of manager and one of
their employees and later altered into the final interview guides (one for managers and one for
employees) that were used throughout the data gathering process. The content and process of
formulating the interview guides will be described in further detail later in this section.
Variations between the interviews were determined by the flow of the conversations. For example,
additional questions were asked to probe answers and explore aspects relevant to the research
topic, which were not covered by the interview guide. Moreover, the interviewees were able to
lead the conversation into previously unconsidered areas. This was essential for the interviewers’
understanding of the topic.
Qualitative data collection and documentation
For this thesis, 15 semi-structured interviews were conducted over a period of seven weeks
with the aim of collecting sufficient data to address the research question through qualitative
analysis. Eight managers, making out the majority of information, and seven employees were
interviewed - one direct employee from each manager. The employees were used as validation
for the managers’ statements.
The interviews were conducted with first-time daily managers (FTDM) from six different
knowledge-based organizations (KBO) coming from five different fields including law, quality
assurance, information technology, municipal administration and design. Each interview was
approximately one hour in length (see specific interview lengths in table 3), some were conducted
in person and others virtually over Teams and were all recorded for transcription. Below, table
3 is displayed to give an overview of the most important demographics and information on
the manager interviewees. Furthermore, the table is split into the preliminary interview that
was conducted and the final data gathering interviews. A table has not been made for the
employee-interviewees as these are not the main subject of the investigation.
Page 31 of 238

managers in the data collection to keep them anonymous. This will be used throughout the
thesis. The next column displays gender and the following gives information on their profession.
The next three columns display management tenure, hybrid management tenure and number of
employees for each interviewee. The last two columns display information about the interview,
if it was held online or in person and the duration of the interviews.
