For all online interviews, video conferencing was used to make sure that facial expressions
were visible to the interviewers to build a connection between the interviewer and interviewees.
Online interviews are often criticized as having lower quality than in-person interviews due to
fewer social cues being transmitted virtually. Others argue that it is in fact possible to build
considerable empathy between interviewer and interviewee through video conferencing and it
has even been suggested that the anonymity of online interviews can facilitate a more open and
honest response (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 350).
The interviews were recorded using Microsoft Teams to make sure that they were available
for later transcription. This was done so the interviewers could focus on the information that
Page 32 of 238
==PAGE:42==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
was given and ask follow-up questions when something needed explanation, clarification or
proved to be a relevant topic to explore further. This would also provide the possibility of a more
detailed coding and following analysis, as the data was available in its entirety throughout the
thesis work and could be used as a means for the authors in familiarizing themselves with the
data before the coding and analysis (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 652, 339). All interviewees were
asked if they consented to the recording of the interview before beginning. The recordings were
downloaded and saved in a Microsoft Teams folder so that they could be accessed later, when
the transcription was done.
A role was assigned for each interviewer in all interviews - one would ask questions and the
other would take notes, even though there would be a recording. The person in charge of the
interview had multiple responsibilities during the interview. They would introduce the aim and
scope of the project for the interviewee to make sure they knew the direction the interview was
going. They would also ask for consent to record the interview and inform about the anonymity
of the gathered data in the introduction of the interview. Moreover, they would make sure that
the interview was on schedule and that all relevant questions were asked and answered. The
person in charge of taking notes would, apart from noting down essential takeaways, be able to
ask questions that they felt were not answered or needed to be clarified.
Partial transcriptions were made of all interviews, where the notes were used as a framework.
Each interview was gone through in its entirety, and then all quotes that could prove relevant
for the coding and following analysis were noted down in the relevant place of the notes. These
transcriptions are all included in Appendix D. It was decided by the authors that it would be better
to spend the time needed to include all relevant quotes, rather than missing out on important
statements, than saving time on this step of the work. This meant that much time was spent on
the transcriptions, but as the essence of the interviews is drawn out from coding and analyzing
the interviews, it is believed to have been worth it.
All interviews were conducted in Danish. Therefore, all quotes displayed in the thesis are trans-
lated by the authors. The quotes used for the analysis were all kept in the original language for
the analysis and only translated when drawn into the report so that minimal meaning would be
lost.
Interview guide
Two interview guides were formulated for the semi-structured interviews - one for managers
and one for employees, which are displayed in Appendix A.3. An interview guide is used as a
guide for the interviewer to make sure they stay on track but is not restrictive to the questions
and topics that can be discussed in the interview.
The process of formulating the final interview guides consisted of a literature search to form
preliminary interview guides (one for managers and one for employees), which were then tested
Page 33 of 238
==PAGE:43==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
on one manager (Interviewee 0) and one employee respectively and then altered based on this to
be used for the final data collection. The focus in this process was on the interview guide for
the managers, and based on this was the employees interview guide formulated. The interviews
based in the preliminary interview guides were carried out in an open manner to explore if there
were other aspects of the topic that were relevant to further explore.
The interview guide consisted of both open and closed questions. Closed questions can be used
to obtain specific information or confirm facts whereas open questions will allow participants to
define and describe things in their own words. This will encourage the interviewees to provide
an extensive answer (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 337).
The closed questions were about factual aspects such as "what is your position?", "how long
have you been a manager?" or "how many employees do you have?". Furthermore, the closed
questions were also about how their organization had implemented a hybrid work model, like
"what is the framework for working hybrid in your organization?". The closed questions would
also include asking about the interviewees’ feelings or thoughts towards specific subjects like
"how do you make sure that your employees are coordinated with each other?" or "what is the
framework for communication within your team?". These questions were formulated, because
certain information was necessary to collect in order to understand the situation of each manager.
Moreover, to investigate how they each approached tasks that were found in the literature to
be essential when managing a hybrid team (for instance how they deal with communication,
coordination and trust). The last aspect of the closed questions related to the managers’ values
and leadership. Included herein were questions such as "what values do you have as a manager
and do you feel that these are reflected in your team?" and "how would you describe your
management style?". These were formulated to get a sense of how they viewed themselves as
managers and how they would approach their team.
The open questions in the interview guide were the questions related to the exploratory part of the
study, focusing on the experiences managers face when managing hybrid teams. These include
questions like "what do you see as the biggest challenges, when managing a hybrid team?" and
"what do you see as the biggest advantages when managing a hybrid team?". Furthermore, the
interviewees were asked to describe how a normal day looks for them when WFH or at the
office and if they feel like they perform equally as a manager for those who WFH and those
who work in the office. This was also an open question as it conveys the interviewee to reflect
on their daily routines. It was asked to get them to open up at an early stage of the interview
about something that comes easy for them to talk about. Moreover, the things that were shared
as answer to this question also had the potential to shed light on insights into relevant aspects
that the interviewers would not know to ask about. In this situation, the interviewers had the
possibility to ask probing questions and dig deeper into interesting topics brought forth by the
Page 34 of 238
==PAGE:44==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
interviewee. Probing questions were in general used throughout the interview as a way to explore
responses of significance to the research topic or seek an explanation to understand the meaning
of a statement of the interviewees (Saunders et al., 2007, p. 338).
Interviewees
After phase 1 in the thesis process, there was an overall idea of the scope of the project from
which the population of interest was formulated as being daily managers, but not having any
specification further than this. Based on this scope, a post was put up on LinkedIn by the
authors and the supervisor Christine Ipsen was tagged to reach a larger group of relevant people.
The context of this text is found in Appendix A.1. This approach of reaching out to relevant
interviewees resulted in one manager (Int 0). The interview with Int 0 and their employee would
provide insights to reformulate both the research question and interview guides. The managers of
interest for the research were redefined by formulating more specific criteria to ensure relevance
of the data and comparability between the interviews. The criteria were that the managers must
• be daily managers of a team in a knowledge-based organization,
• have 4-10 employees in their team,
• manage knowledge workers,
• be first-time managers with a management tenure of no more than four years, and
• currently be the manager of a hybrid team.
It was decided to collect data from a minimum of six managers and six employees for the final
data, as this would potentially result in reaching data saturation but also leave time to transcribe
and analyze the gathered data within the time frame. In the end, seven managers were collected
for the primary data collection.
After formulating the criteria and setting a goal for the data size, interviewees were acquired
through the authors’ network. An email was formulated, stating the purpose of the thesis, the
criteria for the managers, the length of the interview and our need to interview one employee.
Through their network, the authors managed to collect four interviewees (Int 1, 2, 3, 6 and 7) and
the rest (Int 4 and 5) were collected using the snowballing method, where Int 4 was collected
through Int 1 and Int 5 was collected through Int 3. After interviewing each manager, they were
asked if they would provide an employee for an interview, and the employee-interviewees were
therefore chosen by each manager themselves.
3.6
Qualitative data analysis method
The interviews were transcribed into quotes and used as data for the qualitative analysis. The
qualitative analysis consisted of five overall steps enabling the authors to dive deeper into the
Page 35 of 238
==PAGE:45==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
data and move closer to generate meaning. These steps are illustrated in Figure 7. In the figure,
each red square box is one step of the analysis, numbered 1-5. The surrounding gray boxes
contain the methods for each step. Under each of the boxes for step 2-5 is an oval box. These
contain the tactics, which were used for generating meaning in each step. The methods and
tactics used for the analysis were taken from Miles, Huberman, and Saldana (2020). The details
of each step are presented in the following sections.
Screenshots from the analysis made in Miro for each step are included in Appendix A.4-A.10.
Figure 7: The qualitative analysis process including methods and tactics.
Step 1: First cycle coding
The first step of the analysis consisted of first cycle coding. This was done to structure the data
and make sense of it before going into further analysis and drawing out patterns. Codes are
"labels that assign symbolic meaning to the descriptive or inferential information compiled
during a study" (Miles et al., 2020, p. 62), which are assigned to chunks of data. The purpose
of coding the data was both to structure the data and make it more accessible for later analysis,
and was a part of the analysis in itself. By assigning codes to data, a deep reflection about and
interpretation of the data took place, which is a part of the analysis in itself (Miles et al., 2020, p.
63).
The approach towards distributing all quotes into codes was to use the method of partially
ordered meta-matrix to structure the interview-data. This method was used as it is good for large
amounts of data collected in cross-case analyses. It gives an overview of all the data, visualizes
what each bit of data relates to - making it possible to compare across cases - and still keeps the
information of which case the data-bits belong to from.
Instead of ordering the data in a table format, as suggested by Miles et al. (2020, p. 131), it was
structured within boxes using the online collaborative whiteboard platform, Miro. Using the
table structure, each row would represent an interviewee and the columns would be relevant
categories (codes) to divide the data into. Instead, a box was made to resemble each column and
the quotes made by the interviewees were given a color to represent who said the quote. The
Page 36 of 238
==PAGE:46==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
reason behind the change in visually structuring was the large amount of data. Using the boxes
in Miro gave it a dynamic dimension, where each quote could easily be placed within any box,
and subsequent partitioning and clustering was made more intuitive as the boxes could easily be
moved around.
The first cycle coding started with a list of eight a priori (deductive) codes, which were made
based on what literature had emphasized in relation to managerial challenges of a HWM. The
a priori codes were used as a starting point for the analysis. During this step of the analysis,
each quote was given a code by noting patterns. If none of the exciting codes were fitting to a
quote, a new code was made to describe the content of the quote. These new codes are inductive
codes, as they were formulated from the meaning of the quotes. The types of inductive codes
that resulted from the analysis were descriptive codes - summarizing the meaning of the codes
in short phrases - and in vivo codes - using short phrases from the interviewees own language as
codes. Some codes were made into sub-codes, if they were in some way underlying subjects
to a more overall code. The inductive codes made out the largest part of the analysis due to the
exploitative nature of the study.
The first cycle coding resulted in ten overall codes (these will be called level 1 codes going
forward), 13 sub-codes (these will be called level 2 codes going forward), and 13 sub-sub-codes
(these will be called level 3 codes going forward)
Step 2: Second cycle coding
The next step of the qualitative analysis was the second cycle of coding. This step took the
codes from the first cycle of coding and refined them through partitioning and clustering. Each
quote was reevaluated to either let it stay in the assigned code, or consider if it fitted better
within another code or if a new code was needed. In this step, some codes were merged to create
new and more telling codes and others were divided to gain a higher degree of detail when
needed. All new codes made within this step were inductive codes as they would arise from the
exploration of the interview-data. Interpretation and sense making of the data were achieved
in the analytical process of noting patterns followed by continuous clustering and partitioning
back and forth. This lead to new patterns emerging and finally arriving at a structure that divided
the interview-data satisfyingly into codes. The patterns were observed based on the topics within
the quotes. The resulting cluster of codes are depicted in Figure 8. This figure shows a snapshot
from Miro where the analysis was conducted. Each box is a code and they all have different
colors that categorize them into levels: level 1 codes are orange, the level 2 codes are blue, the
level 3 codes are red and the level 4 codes are green. Within each code lies a collection of quotes
from the manager interviewees. The small colored boxes inside each box are the quotes from
the respective managers relating to the given code. The abbreviations for the level 1 codes are
written above each cluster in the figure. The full name of each code and sub-codes are included
in Table 4, which gives an overview of the codes from Figure 8.
Page 37 of 238
==PAGE:47==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
Figure 8: Snapshot of final clusters from the partially ordered meta-matrix analysis.
Step 3: Finding challenges
In the next step of the analysis, the aim was to look at all the codes and extract interview-data that
indicated challenges regarding managing a team within a HWM. Herein, the clusters of codes
were used to extract common challenges across managers. After extracting all interview-data
that indicate challenges, different pieces of data were compared and held together with other
pieces of data to see if they relate to the same challenge(s) (noting patterns). This was done until
all data had been gone through and as many challenges as possible were extracted from the data.
After formulating the initial challenges, these were refined and reformulated until encapsulating
the entirety of what the data showed to be challenging.
Then the content-analytic meta matrix method (Miles et al., 2020, p. 142) was used to compile the
challenges mentioned by interviewees to make an initial exploratory analysis of the challenges.
Herein, it was determined how many of the cases showed similar conclusions, thus subsuming
particular into general. In the table, the challenges were organized on two dimensions:
1. Individual Challenges vs. Organizational Challenges
2. Themes from the analysis
Dividing the challenges into individual and organizational challenges follows the framework
from Kurkland and Bailey (1999). Remember that this framework was also used in section 2.2.2
to make the table of advantages and challenges found in literature (Table 2).
Page 38 of 238
==PAGE:48==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
In the content-analytic meta matrix, the challenges were summarized to give an overview of these
and show how they relate to the above mentioned dimensions. In this step, the case specifics were
deliberately dropped to see the main trends across the cases. In relation to this, counting was
used to see how many interviewees who mentioned each challenge. This number was included
in the table to create an overview of which challenges were noted across cases and which were
only mentioned by a single interviewee. From the analysis, four key challenges were found,
which were used throughout the remainder of the analysis.
Step 4: Connecting challenges
In step 4, the challenges mentioned by more than one interviewee were displayed together with
the information about which interviewee they were experienced by. This made it possible making
comparisons of the challenges across cases. This was used as a first step towards investigating
whether the type of HWM would explain the challenges experienced by FTDMs. Next, the
key challenges found in step 3 were used as a starting point to find connections between the
challenges to investigate, how they were interrelated, and whether they might have an influence
of one another. This was done by using the method of making causal network: cross-case
analysis (Miles et al., 2020, p. 248). To make this analysis, all challenges were taken into account
(also those mentioned only once). Each challenge was compared to the rest and if one had an
influence of the other, they would be connected by an arrow. This step of the analysis was done
to see how the challenges were connected and gain a more holistic view of the challenges for a
FTDM. Understanding the connections, would also give a higher understanding of each isolated
challenge as it adds a degree of detail that would otherwise not be visible. The conclusions from
the causal networks were drawn by making comparisons between the different statements from
the interviewees.
Step 5: Finding explanations
In step 5 of the analysis, it was sought to explain why the challenges were experienced for the
FTDM and investigate whether this could be explained by their type of HWM. This was done as
a part of the analysis of the causal networks, where the findings from the previous steps were
taken into account and summarized with the findings from the causal networks.
3.7
Validation
To validate the qualitative study, a validation matrix was made, which looked at Confirmability,
Reliability, Credibility (internal validity), Transferability (external validity) and Application
(Miles et al., 2020, p. 305-308). The full validation matrix is included in Appendix A.11. The
validation shows that most aspects are met within the qualitative study and report thereof. The
exceptions are: triangulation, intercoder agreements, robustness check and ethical concerns.
These are gone through briefly. Triangulation entail using multiple methods to collect data for
Page 39 of 238
==PAGE:49==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
the same analysis or using multiple methods to analyze for the same data. This was not done in
the study. Intercoder agreements were not set up as the qualitative analysis was made primarily
by one author. A robustness check was not carried out through replication of findings. This was
due to the time-constraint of the thesis work. Finally, ethical concerns were not raised as these
were not viewed as a relevant issue for either data collection or analysis.
3.8
Secondary data
The secondary data for this thesis consists of quantitative data from a survey, designed by
Kirchner, Ipsen, and Andersen (2022) and was collected by a Danish market research company
called Epinion. By collecting the data through Epinion, it was ensured that the survey sample
represented the Danish population. The data was collected online in Denmark for six different
periods between May 2020 and November 2021 which were periods of different stages of COVID-
19 restrictions. Kirchner et al. (2022) has made a description of the COVID-19 restrictions present
in the different periods and the characteristics of the danish society in that period, which is
included in Appendix C.1.
The survey consists of 23 questions asking about people’s experiences of WFH as well as the per-
ceived advantages and disadvantages of working fully or partly from home, the communication
tools used and demographic information. Most of the questions are Likert scale (1 is strongly
disagree and 5 is strongly agree), the demographic questions are either ordinal or nominal and
finally some of the questions are open-ended questions.
The authors received the data on October 12th 2022 from supervisor Christine Ipsen, containing
87 variables and 8176 observations.
3.9
Quantitative method using CRISP-DM
This project involves a suggested solution using machine learning (ML) algorithms to predict
future working arrangements. In order to structure the process to be reliable and efficient, the
CRISP-DM (Cross Industry Standard Process for Data Mining) framework is applied. The
framework is a commonly used tool when carrying out data mining projects as it is independent
of both the industry and the technology used and can therefore standardize projects (Wirth &
Hipp, 2000).
The CRISP-DM process model consists of six phases, which can be seen in Figure 9, and each
phase consists of a number of steps. The order of the phases is not strict, which means the arrows
only indicates the most important and frequent dependencies between the phases. Some of the
phases can be revisited, hence the double arrows, which will be explained later.
Page 40 of 238
==PAGE:50==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
Figure 9: CRISP-DM model
In the first phase Business understanding the project’s objectives and requirements are identified
from a business perspective, then formulated into a data mining problem definition and finally
an initial project plan is established to accommodate the objectives. The next phase Data
understanding is closely linked to the previous phase, as it require some knowledge of the
data to formulate the data mining problem definition and project plan. This phase includes the
process of data collection and getting to know the data and its quality. The third phase Data
preparation involves all actions related to preparing the final data set to use in the analysis:
cleaning, feature selection and constructing new attributes. In most cases it can be necessary to
reenter this phase from modeling in order to restructure the data, and is therefore a recursive
phase. In the next phase Modeling, different modeling techniques are selected and then applied
to the data. Often multiple techniques can be applied to the same data mining problem and some
techniques need the data to be in a specific format. When the models have been developed and
optimized they will be evaluated in the Evaluation phase. This phase secures that the established
business objectives are fulfilled and all business issues have sufficiently been considered. In the
final phase, Deployment, the results and the gained knowledge is organized and presented to the
users in a user-friendly way. How it is presented depends on the individual project; it can be as
simple as a report or implementing a complex data mining process.
In the next sections, the steps taken in each phase in the proposed solution will be presented.
Business Understanding
The first phase, Business Understanding, initiates the quantitative perspective in this thesis and
is fundamental to understand why this part of the research is relevant and how it can create value.
This part of the thesis proposes a solution to one identified challenge from the qualitative analy-
sis. Therefore, before this phase is introduced, the scope of the quantitative analysis have been
already been justified. This is done to understand why the proposed solution (the quantitative
Page 41 of 238
==PAGE:51==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
study) is conducted, what is expected from the solution and what resources are required to make
the solution. Therefore, this phase focuses more on the success criteria that can be established,
both for the business aspect in terms of the usefulness of the ML model, and technical success
criteria. It is important to establish success criteria as they are the standards of which the project
in the end will be considered as a successful solution or not.
Data Understanding
The first step in Data Understanding is to collect data. The quantitative data is developed by
Kirchner et al. (2022), who designed the survey, and then the organization Epinion conducted the
survey. Epinion ensured the data was representative for a diverse work force regarding gender,
age, occupation, education, and so on. Before the data was received, it was gone through by
another DTU master student, thus the data quality was already high when the data was received.
Therefore data wrangling5 was limited. The next steps in the Data Understanding phase were
performed by using quantitative methods in the programming language Python.
First, the data was imported to Python and then explored by an overview of all the variables.
For all the numeric variables, an overview was made which included the variable’s minimum,
maximum, median, average, and standard deviation. This provided an overview of all the
variables, and since the outline of the thesis already was made at this point, some variables
and observations could be removed to simplify further analysis. Next, the quality of data was
investigated, which in this case, only included detecting missing values as the data already
was cleaned. Finally, multiple descriptive analysis’ was conducted in terms of visualizations of
demographic variables, challenges and benefits of working hybrid and the target variable.
Together with Business understanding, we now had an complete overview of the data, and an
idea of how the data should be modified to be used in the models. The knowledge gained in
this phase was essential in the data preparations phase to make qualified decisions before the
modeling phase.
Data Preparation
Having accomplished the phases of scoping the project and an understanding of the data, this
information was utilized to prepare the data for modeling. The first step made in the Data
Preparation phase was final removal of irrelevant variables. Most of these were already removed
in the last phase, but a few needed to be excluded. The next step was cleaning the data, which
ensures the quality of data improves. This only included handling of missing values.
The following step was constructing data, which involved using information from the previous
phases to construct new relevant variables from the data or restructure the variables. An example
5Data wrangling is the process of cleaning, organizing and structuring raw data to make it easier to analyze.
Page 42 of 238
==PAGE:52==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
of this was converting categorical variables into numerical by one-hot encoding6, as the model
interprets these values easier.
Since the data consisted of over 50 variables, a specific number of variables were selected to enter
the model, these are called features, to avoid over-fitting and to increase the prediction power.
The feature selection was done by using Recursive Feature Elimination (RFE). This approach,
as the name suggest, recursively removes one feature at a time, that is the least important to the
model in terms of accuracy, until a given number of features are left. The features left, are the
features that the algorithm selects to explain the target value most accurate.
This phase was revisited several times in the next step.
Modeling
In the Modeling phase, the main goal is to find insightful patterns in data which can be interpreted
into recommendations for the FTDM. In this thesis, we aim to investigate if ML can be used
to successfully describe the number of days employees of knowledge work prefer to work in
regards to working on-site or from home. To do so, the variable ’From now on, if your workplace
allows it, how many days per week would you like to work from home?’ is used as the target
variable. It contains the values: 0 days, 1 day, 2 days, 3 days, 4 days and 5 days, which are the
number of days a respondent wishes to WFH. These are referred to as (prediction) classes.
The first step before selecting the models, is to identify how the data should be divided. When
using ML algorithms, the data is divided into training data and test data. The training data is the
input of the model, and from which the model learn. The test data is kept out of the model, and
used to evaluate how well the model can predict data it has never seen. The training set consisted
of 70% of the data that was randomly selected, and the test size was the remaining 30% of the
data. The percentages of the train and test set are the most common percentages to split the data
into.
In the descriptive analysis, it was identified that the target variable was imbalanced, which
means a big part of the observations belong to only one class. The most common techniques for
handling imbalanced data is under-sampling or over-sampling. When under-sampling, random
observations are removed until all classes contain the same number of observations the smallest
class contain. Using the under-sampling approach, important information can potentially be
removed. Therefore over-sampling will be used. When over-sampling the data, an build-in
Python package selects random information from the small classes, and duplicates these random
observations until the number of observations matches the largest class. This is only done on the
training data, so the test data still reflects the original data.
6One-hot encoding is a conversion of a categorical variable into a format that a machine learning algorithm can
handle. The variable are often made into multiple binary variables.
Page 43 of 238
==PAGE:53==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
Next was choosing which models to use. In this process, six different models were considered
and implemented. The six models that have been tested were Decision Tree Classifier, Random
Forest Classifier, XGBoost Classifier, Naive Bayes Classifier, Support Vector Machines Classifier
and Logistic Regression Classifier. The final two models, which fundamentals have already been
explained in Section 2.7 were Random Forest (RF) Classifier and Naive Bayes (NB) Classifier.
No single ML algorithm dominates, when deciding which to use. The two final algorithms
were chosen, as they are some of the best performing algorithms to a classification problem.
Furthermore, they are theoretically very different, and therefore might capture patterns in the
data, the other model would not. This does not mean that some of the discarded models did not
perform as well as the chosen, but was theoretically similar to one of the chosen models.
One of the reasons, the RF Classifier was selected, was it have build-in methods to handle
unbalanced data. That does not mean, it balances the data, but it tries to minimize the overall
error rate. Furthermore, the algorithm is indifferent to non-linear features and can handle both
binary, categorical and numerical features. Finally, it is an ensemble method, which means it
combines other ML algorithms (decision tree), to generate more accurate predictions.
The Naive Bayes Classifier was selected as it does not require much training data, and since the
final data contains only 1054 observations, this an advantage. Furthermore, it is not sensitive to
irrelevant features, and it can handle both binary, categorical and numerical features.
The RF Classifier were optimized by hyperparameter tuning 7 to improve the performance of the
model. The Naive Bayes Classifier does not allow for hyperparameter tuning, as it is build upon
probabilities. If we knew probabilities of the target variable before collecting the data, these
could be included in the model, but that is not the case.
Evaluation
In the fifth phase, Evaluation, the two different models were evaluated according to Accuracy,
Confusion Matrix and F1-score, which all have been explained in Section 2.7, as the first step in
Evaluation. As earlier mentioned, these evaluation metrics are highly used when working with
multi-class classification problem.
The next step in this phase is to review the process based on the results. Since all models scored
fairly low in all evaluations metrics, the next phase to initiate was not Deployment but revisiting
the Business Understanding phase. Before revisiting Business Understanding, one model was
selected, RF Classifier with over-sampling. In Business Understanding, it was discussed how the
model could be improved with the given data. This lead to reevaluating how the classes of the
dependent variable were categorized. Two new categorizations of the variable were implemented,
merging Class 1 and 2, and Class 3 and 4. This means most of the previous phases were revisited.
7Hyperparameter tuning finds a set of parameters in the model that increase the performance of the learning
algorithm
Page 44 of 238
==PAGE:54==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
Business Understanding to understand from an overall perspective how it would make sense to
change the dependent variable. Data Preparation to restructure the target variable. Modeling to
create the new model, and finally Evaluation to investigate the performance of the new model.
Deployment
The final phase, Deployment, finalizes the development of the project and the solution are often
implemented or presented. In this thesis, as there are no collaboration with an organization or a
specific client, the end product is this thesis report. In the discussion chapter, the aspects required
to implement the solution will be discussed as well as if the solution even can be used. Therefore,
there will not be a Deployment section in Section 6, it will be integrated in the discussion chapter.
3.10
Literature search
The following section explains the process of finding relevant literature for the thesis. The
literature study was an important element when framing the thesis to investigate what already
exists on the topic and explore the gaps. To ensure a transparent process, the seven steps of the
Comprehensive Literature Review developed by Onwuegbuzie and Frels (2016) are used.
The first step, Exploring Beliefs and Topics, relates to the knowledge the authors already have.
In this process, material from former courses taken at Technical University of Denmark were
utilized, which included course materials such as books, articles, slides and lectures from relevant
courses. The second step, Initiating the Search, included developing multiple search strings. The
following search string was one of the first created:
(“Remote work” OR “Working from home” OR “WFH” OR “Telework” OR “Hybrid work”
OR “Working hybrid”) AND (“Distance management” OR “Managing distance work” OR
“Managing remote work” OR “Remote management” OR “Hybrid management” OR
“Workplace strategy” OR “management”) AND (“Productivity” OR “Demotivated”) AND
(“Challenge” OR “problem” OR “Worry” OR “Complication” OR “Drawback”)
Another search string that focuses on the technical aspect of the thesis:
(“machine learning” OR “Predict* model” OR “ML”) AND (”Remote work*” OR “Working
from home” OR “WFH” OR “Telework” OR “Hybrid work*” OR “Working hybrid” OR
“Tele-work*”) AND (“Challenge*” OR “problem*” OR “Worry*” OR “Complication*” OR
“Drawback*”)
A last example, is one of the search strings regarding challenges FTDM can experience:
(“First time” OR “first-time”) AND (“first-line manager” OR ‘daily manager’ OR “FLM”)
AND (“Challenge” OR “problem” OR “Worry” OR “Complication” OR “Drawback”) AND
(“remote work*” OR “hybrid work*” OR “Work* from home” OR “telework*”)
Page 45 of 238
==PAGE:55==
3
METHODOLOGY
Master Thesis
These search string have been modified in numerous ways to expand the search or if search less
results, but these are the most common searches used. The words used in the search strings are
derived from literature found in the first step as well as from this step after the initial explorations.
The third step, Storing and Organizing Information, the literature found in step 2 should be
stored and organized. This was done in Excel in Microsoft Teams, so all the knowledge gained
could be shared. In Excel, the following information of the literature was inserted: a link of
the literature, the title, a short description of the main points, quotes, key-words, year, which
research question it could be relevant for, the search string, additional comments, and if the other
author should read it as well.
The fourth step, Selecting/Deselecting Information, was repeated multiple times, when new or
more literature or information was found.
The fifth step, Expanding the Search, the literature search was expanded by using multiple
MODES, which includes Media, Observations, Document, Experts and Secondary data. The
elements of the MODES that has been used are lectures, interviews, articles, books and secondary
data. The literature was obtained from Scopus, the Royal Library of Copenhagen, DTU Findit,
Google Scholar and relevant web pages. When obtaining literature from Google Scholar and
especially non-academic web pages a thorough validation of the site and author (when possible)
was conducted. When all the final literature was selected and stored, step six could be initiated.
The sixth step, Analyzing/Synthesizing Information, all the information gained from the litera-
ture was connected to the analysis of both the qualitative and quantitative study.
The last step, step seven, Presenting the CLR Report, is finalized when the report is submitted.

Empirical findings
This section presents an initial analysis of the primary data, which entails an overview of
the codes that emerged from the interview-data. In this lies an interpretation of and meaning-
generation of the data through noting patters, clustering and partitioning. Quotes from the
collected data are presented throughout to explain the reasoning behind the codes and clustering.

EMPIRICAL FINDINGS

As described in Section 3.6, the gathered interview-data was structured using the approach of a
partially ordered meta matrix. After engaging in some iterations of partitioning and clustering,
the analysis arrived at 28 sub-codes distributed across five level 1 codes, making it a total of 33
codes. The data within each code is later used in the analysis to synthesize the meaning into
challenges. For each column in Table 4, is a code of a lower level. Thus, the column furthest to
the left holds the overall codes (level 1 codes), the next column holds the level 2 codes, the third
column holds the level 3 codes and the last holds the sub-sub-sub codes (level 4 codes).
4.2
Describing the codes
The following is focused around the level 1 codes (see Table 4), which will be described and
connected to their underlying codes. Each section describes a level 1 code and its relevant
sub-codes and explains the meaning behind their clustering. Only codes that are used in the
analysis of identifying challenges are described in the following, so the reader is not overloaded
with unnecessary information.
Management Responsibilities (MR)
The code denoted Management Responsibilities (MR) collects descriptions from the interviewees
about what their role as managers entails and what they feel their responsibilities are. An example
of a quote within this code comes from Int 4:
"Basically, I believe that a manager must take the necessary actions for the employees to do
their job. That is, get rid of obstacles, make sure they are well, have fun and are happy to be
here." 00.30.03
The cluster that emerged from this is the largest in the analysis, as ’management responsibilities’
is a broad term and encapsulates many aspects of the interview-data.
Planning (PLA)
Planning is one of the four functions of management, which was briefly mentioned in connection
to the role of the FTDM in section 2.1 as an essential aspect of a DM’s role. Planning was a
topic that many of the managers touched upon during the interviews. Some mentioned planning
in relation to challenges that it could bring working within a HWM. An example of this is the
statement from Int 2:
"I think that there is more planning now after Corona and after hybrid management is a reality.
We need to plan more to make it all work. And then there is the thing about scheduling meetings
and figuring out who is in the office and when (...)" 00.53.04
Page 48 of 238
==PAGE:58==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
This code connects to MR as it is a responsibility of the DM to assist in the planning activities
that relate to their team.
Giving Up Responsibility (GUR)
Giving Up Responsibility (GUR) was created because multiple quotes from managers expressed
that some would either not feel like they were being managers for the employees WFH or they
in some way would treat employees differently when WFH. This is exemplified by the quotes by
Int 1 and 3 respectively:
"I am not really a manager for the ones working from home. Many of them, I only see for the
morning meeting nine to nine thirty and then I don’t hear from them again." 00.04.31
"If we do a meeting virtually (...) then I feel, ’OK if you would like to do this virtually then I also
feel that you have the responsibility to make it work’. That way, I will be more active in a
meeting when it is in-person." 00.32.05
These quotes express a tendency for how managers approach their role of managing hybrid
teams, thus relating them to the MR code and giving it a clear relevance to the analysis.
Personal Preference (PEP)
The code denoted Personal Preference (PEP) relates to the planning and scheduling that comes
with a HWM. In this sub-code the statements relate to how the personal preferences of employees
affect the way managers are able to make plans and structure working schedules, and how they
are often met with dissatisfied employees regarding their work arrangements. This is made clear
in the quote by Int 3:
"There are some who feel they work from home too rarely and some who would rather be
without telework. Those who feel that they do not get to telework enough, challenge me on the
rules every time — whether the same rules still apply and that they really feel that those rules
are unreasonable." 00.11.30
Trust (TRU)
Trust (TRU) was found to be a high priority to many of the interviewees. Many of them felt that
especially in a HWM, trust is crucial because they are not able to see, monitor or know what
their employees are doing when WFH. Int 3 stated:
"I have no idea about what my employees do when they work from home and how long they take
to do a task or how long that task should take them. And there are no conditions making it
possible for me to know. It is trust-based management. two hundred percent trust." 00.44.00
Without trust, a HWM would not work according to Int 6:
Page 49 of 238
==PAGE:59==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
"It takes that I have full trust in my employees and that they deliver when working from home.
Otherwise the work model does not work." 00.14.17
Monitoring (MON)
Monitoring (MON) is another one of the four functions briefly mentioned in section 2.1. This
code is connected to the trust or mistrust that the interviewees have when their employees WFH.
Some interviewees have a hard time being comfortable with their inability to monitor their
employees when they WFH, as they feel it is an essential part of their role as managers. This is
made clear by Int 1:
"You cannot execute management without monitoring." 00.55.57
Other managers have contrasting feelings towards monitoring and feel that their time is better
spent on other tasks. To this, Int 6 states:
"There are some departments here that monitor a lot. And we have the tools to do it. I just feel
that my time is better spent on other things." 00.50.11
Meetings and Chats (MC)
The level 1 code denoted Meetings and Chats (MC) collects descriptions from the interviewees
about what types of meetings they have with their employees on a regular basis. Also how people
generally talk to each other around the offices and when WFH. Int 7 talks about the importance
of having regular meetings in the calendar to have an established contact between manager and
employee:
"Typically, I have a meeting booked with each individual employee for a one-on-one as we call
it, so that we can establish a quite close connection on a regular basis. And then they can of
course come to me in addition to that." 00.12.47
This is just one example of an approach towards meetings that some of the interviewees express.
They also talk about the chats that naturally occur around the office.
Availability (AVA)
The availability of both managers and employees was discussed throughout all of the inter-
views. Some interviewees reported a completely unchanged availability for themselves and their
employees when WFH compared to working at the office, illustrated by Int 5:
"Usually, it is just as easy to get a hold of people when they work from home as when they sit at
their desk here in the office." 00.15.49
Page 50 of 238
==PAGE:60==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
Others reported a general feeling in the office that people are less available when not being
co-located. Int 6 expressed:
"(...) You need to be available. Well, I am also available when working from home — you can
just call. It takes some getting used to I think. And people need to get used to calling each other
up I think. They need to figure out that it is just like walking into someone’s office — it is not
dangerous and it is not different from that." 00.34.10
Int 2 even reported a higher availability from those at home:
"There is less disturbances when people work from home. When you are in the office, you talk in
crisscross, trow around with paper planes and all that kind of stuff. You don’t do that at home.
At home you are available when you are available. That makes it easier to get a hold of people
one-on-one. You don’t need to first book a meeting room and all that." 00.35.52
In Person, Remote or Hybrid (IRH)
In the interviews it was often discussed whether some meetings or conversations were better
suited to do in person, and all interviewees had position on which things were best suited to
do in person, online or as a mix. From this, the code In Person, Remote or Hybrid (IRH) was
formed to collect the attitudes towards the topic. For instance, all interviewees reported that they
prefer doing one-on-one meetings face-to-face. Like the statement from Int 2:
"We have a one-on-one once a week and for that I prefer it if we are on site because it just works
best to do one-on-ones face-to-face." 00:05:09
Most interviewee also have other aspects that influence a feeling that some meetings are best
kept on-site. This is exemplified by statement from Int 7 and 4 respectively:
"It depends on the nature of the meeting and on the importance of the meeting." 00.28.56
"When the bigger decisions need to be made (...) then it is definitely best to be on-site I think.
Then I can better interpret people’s reactions to some things" 00.41.56
Behavior During Meetings (BDM)
The next sub-code concerns how people behave during meetings — be they in person, hybrid or
remote. All managers report feeling a difference between how their employees and themselves
behave when the meetings are in person contra when all or some of the participants join online.
This shows in the statements from Int 1 and 7 respectively:
"I have an expectation that the person working from home is more active in meetings (...), again
it is about body language, interpretations and presence. (...) If you join from home there are
certain things you must compensate for. Therefore, you need to be more active when you are at
home." 00.45.03
Page 51 of 238
==PAGE:61==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
"Generally for meetings conducted via Teams and meetings where everyone is physically present
in the same room there is a difference [in people’s behavior]. And I most certainly prefer the
latter." 00.35.39
Knowledge Sharing (KNS)
Knowledge Sharing (KNS) entails all the things that managers and employees talk about on
a daily basis, which contribute to their ability to do their respective jobs. This includes both
manager to employee, employee to manager and employee to employee. Most interviewees
comment on the issues that lie in connection to knowledge sharing in a HWM. Some feel that
the knowledge sharing can only take place in the office, like Int 5:
"That opportunity for knowledge sharing and the possibility that you hear something, where you
can contribute with something valuable is not there when you work from home." 00.40.53
While others feel like it can be done just as easily from home — it just takes some getting used
to, like Int 6
"I think that for many, there are some things that you don’t get around to call or write to
someone about. I do that myself. (...) I think it is because it just feels more natural to walk up to
someone." 00.34.42
"Meeting Culture Has Gone Crazy" (MGC)
This last relevant code in the cluster is about the meeting culture that a HWM brings. Not all
managers mentioned this in their interview, but those who did, commented on the extend to
which meetings have taken over peoples’ calendars and in some ways taken away the spontaneity
of the way people interact in the workplace. According to Int 7
"It takes away a certain spontaneity." 00.17.15
Int 2 goes in the same direction and states:
"The calendars have been blocked up and the meeting culture has gone crazy. Many of the small
things are now taking up half an hour instead. (...) There is a tendency that things, which should
not have been meetings, have become meetings." 00.37.19
Success of a HWM (SH)
The level 1 code denoted Success of a HWM (SH) does not include any quotes. Instead it is an
overall category, which connects its sub-codes under the same overall theme relating to how to
make a HWM successful. The codes that are included in this cluster are all elements that the
interviewees mentioned to have an essential connection to whether a HWM would function or not.
Page 52 of 238
==PAGE:62==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
Technology and Facilities (TAF)
When the interviewees talk about the technological facilities of their work spaces and home
offices, there is no doubt that this is an essential aspect of having a successfully implemented
HWM. According to interview-data, there is no foundation for conducting hybrid meetings and
ensuring a connection between the work place and people’s homes without strong technological
facilities. As stated by Int 6:
"It [hybrid work] necessitates that the technology keeps up. There is nothing worse than going
to a conference room and then it doesn’t work. That just NEEDS TO work." 00.07.18
Moreover, this code encapsulates the physical surroundings of the people in an organization,
including their home-offices. The interviewees work within organizations with quite different
approaches towards accommodating a well-functioning home-office. In some cases, like for Int
7, the organization takes their responsibility very seriously:
"Creating a good home-office is something we take very seriously. (...) If an employee requests a
huge screen or a new chair then it is typically approved right away." 00.13.57
In other cases, like for Int 3, the organization constructs their HWM around the regulations made
about the responsibilities of work-places when it comes to WFH and employees’ home-offices
"There is the Danish Working Environment Act that steps in if employees work from home more
than one day per week. It is among other things because of this regulation that we have the rule
[about only working from home once per week]." 00.25.23
Rules (RUL)
Most of the interviewees comment on the need for setting up clear rules in order to have a
well-functioning HWM — like the statement made by Int 6:
"It takes razor-sharp coordination. Therefore, if you have not set up clear rules and the team do
not agree on them and have not drawn them up collectively, then I believe it will not be
successful." 00.29.00
This code also encompasses when there are rules set up, but the interviewees meet resistance on
them, like for Int 3
"In theory, the rule is that they must make an agreement with their closest manager (me) about
working from home. I have met so much resistance on that so now I have backed as much on the
rule as to agree that if they just send me a text message about where they will be, then I write
back OK."
Page 53 of 238
==PAGE:63==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
Coordination (COO)
The sub-code Coordination (COO) entails the organization- or team-rules made in regard to
coordination. This is included as a sub-code, as some interviewees mentions rules specifically
connected to coordination. Herein, Int 6 comments on the poor technical coordination between
departments:
"We also have Teams, which makes it confusing because then there are some departments, where
they use Teams more than they use Zoom, and then when you call for a meeting it can be either
one or the other." 00.22.45
Organization Guidelines (ORG)
Organizational Guidelines (ORG) collects descriptions from the interviewees about how their
organization has approached the implementation of a HWM. Have they taken a stands on how
the work model should look? Are the guidelines vague or strict? Does it leave the managers
on their own to interpret things or is there enough support from the organization based on the
guidelines they have given? For all interviewees, their organizations have given some degree of
guidelines to go from. Some are quite vague and others more strict. For Int 3, things seem quite
clear, and in contrast, Int 4 experiences that others in the organizations have interpreted the rules
differently:
"It is the CEO who has decided the framework." 00.02.31
"I can see that some of my colleagues have other rules." 00.09.38
In the middle there is Int 7. In their organization there is an overall guideline which is up to
interpretation to an extend where the managers can figure out with themselves and their team
what works best for them:
"Commonly, there is not really general guidelines from top management other that this concept
of "best of both worlds". In that way it is very self-organized for the teams because all teams are
different." 00.14.53
"We Lack Fixed Rules" (WLF)
A result of the vague organizational guidelines that some of the interviewees experience, they
express a feeling of needing more support from higher up the organization in order to justify the
decisions they make. Int 4 is especially uneasy about not having more support:
"We lack fixed rules from the organization’s side i think." 00.09.18
"I do not feel that I am on safe grounds." 00.12.19
Going one step further, the lack of rules given by the organization has resulted in managers
stepping in and creating their own rules to reduce the uncertainties of the HWM. In connection
to this Int 4 continues:
Page 54 of 238
==PAGE:64==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
"(...) such that I do not need to make up my own rules. I would really like it if I could refer to the
company’s own rules. Then it would be easier to argue why things are the way they are."
00.11.32 "I have had to make a rule for my own sake (...) I don’t really know why i made the
rule like that." 00.09.59
Calendars (CAL)
The sub-code Calendars (CAL) is about coordination aspects using calendars to know if people
are at home, at the office or out for a walk with the dog etc. Int 3 illustrates this:
"Generally, they mange it [when to work from home] themselves in the sense that it must be clear
from their calendar where they are, how to reach them and when they are occupied" 00.06.37
Social Needs
Moving on the the next level 1 code denoted Social Needs (SN). This code collects descriptions
from the interviewees about why the social aspects of a work environment are important and
that because of this facet people choose to come in to the office even when they do not have to.
This is pointed out by Int 6:
"I think everybody thrives in having the possibility to work from home. Many, like myself, choose
to work on-site three to four days per week because most people generally like to be close to
each other and look each other in the eye when they say good morning." 00.17.51
Beyond the quotes that relate directly to why social aspects are important for a work environment,
a number of sub-codes emerged that in some way are related to this overall umbrella of social
needs. These codes are given more description in the following.
Forming Social Connections (FSC)
When it comes to Forming Social Connections (FSC) in a HWM, the interviewees quite agree
that this is more difficult to do than it is when everybody is at the office nine to five, five days
a week. The social interactions are less natural online and you need to reach out to each other
more explicitly than just having a chat by the coffee machine. Int 2 states as an example:
"When you are at the office, it happens completely naturally that you talk to people while
grabbing a cup of coffee. Then you get to build those relationships without even noticing. When
you sit at home, you need to create these relations yourself and it becomes a bit artificial"
00.08.58
The interviewees especially find it difficult to create social bonds with new team members. Int 7
phrases this by stating:
Page 55 of 238
==PAGE:65==
4
EMPIRICAL FINDINGS
Master Thesis
"Especially with newly employed team members, who have just been hired and who live
relatively far away from the office so they only come into the office once or twice during the
week. Then it is difficult creating that initial bond with them." 00.18.34
Weakening Social Connections (WSC)
The code Weakening Social Connections (WSC) collects the statements regarding how WFH
weakens the connections and cohesion between team members and how this loss of cohesion
can impact the organization’s ability to retain employees. Int 5 states:
"I think it comes with a cost. The feeling of belonging to something bigger. The sort of
"us"-feeling is harder to keep alive when all the time somebody is working from home." 00.16.45
"If you feel like you could just as well belong somewhere else, then we will begin having a
harder time retaining our employees I think." 00.22.43
"Tuning In to People" (TIP)
"Tuning In to People" (TIP) collects the quotes that are about managers’ need for being in touch
with their employees - both professionally and more personally. The interviewees express a need
to be "tuned in" with their employees to know how they are feeling and they find this more
difficult to do online. Int 4 express these aspects:
"I like to be in touch with my employees to some degree to know how they are feeling, and I think
that is done best in person." 00.08.47
Employees Demand WFH (ED)
The last level 1 code is denoted Employees Demand WFH (ED) and collects descriptions from
the interviewees about their views on how the HWM has been received by employees. Many
experience that employees have a demand for working hybrid and even view it as a competitive
parameter when looking for a job:
"After Corona, the demand for telework has been so great that in some way it has become a
competitive parameter for companies. Regardless whether our top-management agrees with it."
00.18.07
Can People Control It? (CPC)
Even though people have taken the HWM in and many employees feel that it has improved their
work-life, interviewees report that some employees are having issues controlling the freedom.
They are having trouble finding a balance of WFH and going to the office that ensures their
mental health. Int 1 comments on this issue:
"It can be dangerous to let people telework to much. You fall into some sort of safety-bubble at
home, which can be difficult to break out of." 00.38.52
Page 56 of 238
==PAGE:66==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
5
Qualitative Analysis
The previous section (Section 4.1) displayed the codes found in the initial analysis of the
interview-data and structured them in clusters. The clustering made it possible to structure the
data according to the meaning within the data. The clustering and making of codes was an
essential step leading up to beginning the further analysis.
This section analyzes the data within each code displayed in Table 4 to draw out the underlying
meaning behind the statements from the interviews. The analysis from the previous section
resulted in a structure for the collection of quotes derived from the interview-data, and the follow-
ing analysis lies in interpreting the feelings and attitudes expressed through the interview-data.
The analysis generates meaning through the tactics of noting patterns, counting, and making
comparisons until in the end being able to subsume particulars into the general (Miles et al.,
2020).
In this analysis lies an interpretation of and meaning-generation from the data until in the end
being able to subsume particulars into patterns.
The analysis seeks to address research question 2:
Which challenges does a hybrid work model bring for first-time daily managers working within
knowledge-based organizations? And can the type of hybrid work model explain which
challenges first-time daily managers experience when managing a hybrid team?
This question contains two elements - one regarding the general challenges impacting FTDMs’
work in a HWM and the other concerning whether some challenges can be explained by the type
of HWM. These are addressed separately, beginning with the first part. For the first part, data is
structured in a content-analytic summary table, which is an exploratory method for displaying
data. This gives an overview of the challenges found in the analysis and structures it on relevant
dimensions. The result of this first part of the analysis is shown in Table 6. Then the overall
conclusions which can be made from the table are presented in text format. Instead of going into
further details of the challenges right away, the second part of research question 2 is presented.
Due to the large number of challenges, they cannot all be analyzed in detail. Therefore the second
part of research question 2 is investigated to narrow down the challenges by identifying the key
challenges from the interview-data. Subsequently, causal networks are made, summarizing the
key challenges and how these are internally connected to other relevant challenges. Resulting of
this, one key challenge is chosen for the next part of the study, where the aim will be to find a
solution for the chosen challenge.
Page 57 of 238

5.1
Types of HWMs for the interviewees
Information regarding the types of HWMs, which the interviewees from this study work within, is
displayed in Table 5 below. These HWMs can all be classified as one of the three types developed
by Griffis (2021) (presented in Section 2.2.1). These include: office-first (OF), office-occasional
(OO) and remote-first (RF). There are certain aspects, that decide whether a work model is
categorized into each type. This includes the rules about how many days per week employees
are allowed to WFH, why people choose to WFH, and whether management is done primarily
from the office or not. In Table 5, these aspects are displayed for each of the interviewees. The
quotes that underpin the findings in the following are included in Appendix B.1.

extra day some weeks. All these interviewees state that they are not comfortable WFH, as it
makes their work as managers more difficult. When it comes to the employees WFH and what

because they have a task that requires extra concentration. Moving on to the OO HWM, the table

Having established how the HWMs are structured for each of the interviewees, the analysis will
move on to address research question 2 in the following.
5.2
Managerial challenges in a HWM
This step of the analysis will address the first part of research question 2:
Which managerial challenges does a hybrid work model bring for first-time daily managers
working within knowledge-based organizations?
This first part of research question 2 has an exploratory approach to the problem. With this
question, the aim is to find out which challenges FTDMs face when managing a hybrid team
without restricting to a certain type of challenges or in other ways narrow the focus. This way,
the data gets to talk for itself and keeping the bias of the authors low in the beginning of the
analysis.
The content-analytic summary table method is used to analyze the interview-data connected to
the above stated part of research question 2. The results of the analysis are displayed in Table 6.
Recall from the method section (Section 3.6) that the content-analytic summary table displays
all identified challenges and organizes these on two dimensions:
1. Individual Challenges vs. Organizational Challenges
2. Themes from the analysis
The table synthesizes four pieces of information for each cell: 1) The challenges identified from
the interview-data (bullets); 2) How many interviewees reported to experience each challenge
(number in parentheses); 3) The level 1 code that each challenge belongs to (rows) and 4)
Whether the challenge is categorized as individual or organizational (columns). This structure
enables a comparison of occurrence frequency between the identified challenges as well as
a comparison between these challenges and the challenges found in literature (presented in
Table 2). Finally, categorizing the challenges into individual and organizational shows where the

challenges have their influence, an by this take action against them. Moreover, classifying the
challenges into codes gives an overview of what each challenge relates to and illustrates which
of the areas are the most negatively inflicted by a HWM.

The Table 6 shows a total of 29 challenges, which were identified based on interview-data from
the seven manager-interviewees (see Table 3 in the methods section for their details). These
challenges are a representation of the challenges met by a FTDM in relation to their role as
manager for a team of knowledge workers. Some challenges are expressed by only one manager
and some by multiple. Out of these, 16 have been identified as individual challenges experienced
by the manager(s) and the remaining 13 have been identified as organizational challenges. Indi-
vidual challenges are the challenges that lie within the responsibilities of the individual manager,
and thus the challenges that can be solved by the individual manager. Likewise, organizational
challenges are the challenges that lie within the responsibilities of the organization, and thus the
challenges that can be solved further up in the organization.
Some overall reflections are made from taking a first glance at the table before diving into the
details of the challenges found in the analysis. First, the table shows that out of the identified
challenges, six are connected to the MR cluster, six are connection to the MC cluster, seven
are connected to the SH cluster, eight are connected to the SN cluster and two are connected to
the ED cluster. Thus, the most challenges is related to Social Needs. Generally, the challenges
are quite evenly distributed between individual and organizational with MC being an exception.
The cells with the most challenges are individual challenges for the MC cluster and individ-
ual challenges for the SN cluster, which each has five challenges. The cells with the fewest
challenges are organizational challenges for the MC cluster, organizational challenges for the
ED cluster and individual challenges for the ED cluster. The reason for the ED cluster having
much fewer challenges than the rest can be explained by it being much smaller with much fewer
sub-codes, thus not containing much material from the interviews. Therefore, the organizational
challenges for the MC cluster has the relatively fewest challenges. Lastly, the challenges that
have been mentioned by the largest amount of interviewees have been mentioned by five out
of the total seven. This includes four challenges, namely Knowledge sharing is challenges
(organizational MC challenge) Human intuition is challenged (individual SN challenge), Some
things not suited for telework (individual MC challenge) and Customizing work arrangements
for employees(organizational MR challenge).
Now that the challenges from the interview-data have been identified and displayed using the
content-analytic meta matrix in Table 6, it is time to dive further into what lies behind these
challenges. Due to the large number of challenges, they cannot all be emphasized with more
detail in this thesis. In the following section, the challenges are presented in relation to which
HWM that they are each experienced in. To make this analysis a bit more simple, the challenges
which were only mentioned by one interviewee (the ones without a number in Table 6) are
excluded from the following.

5.2.1
Influence of HWM
During the interviews, a pattern emerged regarding the structure of the hybrid work and how this
affected the attitudes from each interviewee towards working within a HWM. The following
analysis seeks to address second part of research question 2:
Can the type of hybrid work model explain which challenges first-time daily managers
experience when managing a hybrid team?
The type of HWM seemed to affect which challenges each interviewee felt. The found patterns
suggested that in general, managers from the OF HWM were quite negative towards the HWM,
whereas managers from OO and RF were more positive and focused mainly on the personal
benefits the model gave them and their employees. In the OF work model they also focused on
the positive things it gave employees, but they did not feel these benefits themselves. They only
felt the difficulties and inconveniences that comes from having a HWM. This may be due to the
fact that the managers within a OF HWM mainly work from the office and are therefore not
able to experience the positive sides of WFH. The attitudes from each interviewee with quotes
that underpin the interpretations are included in Appendix B.2, as the amount of information is
too large to include in the report. Instead, the condensed interpretations of how each manager
views the HWM within their organization are presented shortly in the following before going
into more detail about which specific challenges they each experience.
Int 1 is predominantly negative towards having a HWM. They feel that it gives freedom and a
better WLB to the employees but that it makes the role as DM more difficult. They feel that the
HWM has a cost on the knowledge sharing within the team and weakens the social cohesion of
the employees, which in turn has the possibility of hurting retention.
Int 2 is predominantly positive towards having a HWM. They enjoy the benefits of WFH to a
great extend themselves as it gives freedom, flexibility and a better WLB. Int 2 does however
also experience the cost that the HWM has on the social aspect of the work place.
Int 3 is predominantly negative towards having a HWM. Their attitudes to a great degree re-
sembles those of Int 1. Int 3 also feels that a HWM gives freedom to the employees but that
it makes the role as DM more difficult. They feel that the HWM has a cost on the knowledge
sharing within the team and weakens the social cohesion of the employees, which in turn has the
possibility of hurting retention.
Int 4 is predominantly positive towards having a HWM. They thinks that it works well and
gives employees flexibility. However they do see some challenges in having the more personal
interactions with employees online. Furthermore, Int 4 feels that they need more support from
the organization in relation to the rules connected to the WHM.
Int 5 is predominantly negative towards having a HWM. Their attitudes to a great degree re-
sembles those of Int 1 and Int 3. They feel that it gives freedom to the employees but that it
Page 62 of 238
==PAGE:72==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
makes the role as DM more difficult. They feel that the HWM has a cost on the knowledge
sharing within the team and weakens the social cohesion of the employees, which in turn has the
possibility of hurting retention.
Int 6 is evidently positive towards having a HWM. They think it is great for both employees
and managers and does in the bigger picture not feel that the HWM creates challenges that the
traditional work model did.
Lastly, Int 7 is predominantly positive towards having a HWM. They think it works well for both
employees and themselves as manager and feel that it has some benefits towards the types of
tasks the team has. However, they also feel that meetings are best in person, as they feel there
are some professional aspects that are not possible to include in online meetings.
Noting the patterns of attitudes from the interviewees, led to an interest to investigate if there
is in fact something differentiating the challenges experienced by managers from the different
types of work models. This is done in the following analysis.
The analysis in this section collects all challenges from Table 6 which were mentioned by more
than one interviewee and presents which types of HWMs they are each connected to. The results
are presented in Table 7 and 8. Table 7 displays the challenges which are only reported by
interviewees from one type of HWM and Table 8 displays the challenges which are reported by
interviewees from multiple types of HWMs.
In this part of the analysis, the overall patterns observed from the tables are presented. This will
not go into all challenges but only remark on those that suggest a relationship with one or more
type(s) of HWM(s). Thus, the analysis seeks to give an overview of how the FTDMs within each
HWM experience their role and where the interviews have suggested a difference between the
models.
