The tables suggest that the interviewees with an OF HWM are the ones experiencing the highest
number of challenges and the interviewee with a RF model experiences the lowest number.
Moreover, at least one interviewee from the OF model experiences all of the challenges except
for Overbooked calendars.
Table 7 shows that Int 4 does not report many challenges, but that Int 1, 3 and 5 are distributed on
most of the challenges. In this table, the first three challenges are related to trust and monitoring,
the next three are related to social factors and the rest do not belong to any common category. In
the part of Table 8 with challenges experienced by interviewees from both OF and OO work
models, the first three challenges are related to organizational factors, the next three are related
to social factors (like in Table 7) and the rest do not belong to any common category. The bottom
of Table 8 displays the challenges which are reported by interviewees from all three types of
HWMs. Due to these being experienced by interviewees from all the types of HWMs, these four
challenges are viewed as the key challenges, which will be the focal point of the further analysis.
This means that these four key challenges will be described in further detail in the following
step of the analysis to find out more about how they are experienced by the interviewees. The
four challenges are expressed as the key challenges of the study because they are not specific for
any type of HWM. This means that, when choosing a challenge to solve, it will make sense to
choose one of the key challenges, as the solution will not be restricted to organizations with a
specific type of HWM. This is why they are used as focal point and described in the following.
Because there is no differences in how the key challenges are experienced depending on the
types of work models, this aspect is analyzed by looking at the challenges connected to the key
Page 64 of 238
==PAGE:74==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
challenges, as these are able to give a picture of potential differences between the experience of
the challenges depending on the type of work model.
Going back to the tables above, these show that the interviewee with a RF work model reports
quite few challenges compared to those of both the OF and OO work models, and that, again,
the interviewees with the OF work model report significantly more challenges than those with
the two other types of HWMs. This suggests that it may be more challenging to work within an
OF HWM than within both an OO and a RF HWM. This pattern is illustrated in the following
before moving on to the analysis of the four key challenges.
Int 1, 3 and 5 expressed to some degree that they do not trust their employees when WFH. Some
of this is related to the employees’ productivity when WFH. Int 3 and 5 express a disbelief in
the efficiency of their employees when teleworking as well as a speculation of whether they are
doing other things than working. Int 1 expresses a mistrust related to employees’ motivation
behind wanting to WFH.
The mistrust some managers have towards their employees, may very well be connected to
their inability to see the employees and monitor their performance when teleworking. This
connects the challenges Mistrust in employees and Monitoring challenges, leading trust and
control to have an inter-dependency, which is expressed by the interviewees. Both Int 3 and 5,
who expressed feeling a lack of trust in the productivity of their employees, expressed a loss
of control or a need for setting up ways of monitoring employees. Int 5 wishes more control,
i.e. monitoring opportunities, to be able to know when the employees are working and when
they are not. Int 1 also expresses a need for control, but more indirectly by telling how they get a
sense of how their employees are performing.
The relationship between Mistrust in employees and Monitoring challenges goes both ways,
and is an influencing relation. It seems that managers with low trust in their employees either
feel an urge to monitor their employees or that being unable to see what their employees are
doing, lowers their trust in them. Either way, there seems to be a connection between the two
challenges. Accordingly, Int 6 and 7 both have a high degree of trust in their employees and feel
no need to monitor them, which suggests that the relationship also goes the other way around —
having trust calls for not needing to monitor.
Interviewee 1, 3, 4 and 5 all require their employees to register in their calendars whether they
are WFH or coming to the office and when they are available or taking breaks, running other
errands etc. The interviewees can in this way keep track of their employees, where they are and
when they are working. All the interviewees who use calendar systems to keep track of their
employees have an OF HWM. Two of these interviewees, Int 3 and 5, are the ones that mostly
expressed a lack of trust and need for monitoring. In part, this explains why Int 3 and 5 are also
expressing a clear demand to keep track of their employees from their calendars.
Page 65 of 238
==PAGE:75==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
In contrast, Int 6 who works within a RF model does not know when their employees WFH
either, but because Int 6 has a loose approach towards WFH versus in the office, they do not
view it as a necessity to keep track of their employees’ whereabouts.
This analysis, held together with earlier patterns related to trust, indicate that there is a rela-
tionship between the type of HWM a company has, and how the FTDMs feel in relation to
trust, keeping track of their employees and monitoring. It shows that in an OF HWM, FTDMs
have less trust in their employees when WFH than FTDMs have in a RF HWM. In summary,
these findings indicate that there is a relationship between the type of HWM and the approach
towards monitoring and the need for keeping track of employees’ whereabouts. At least there is
a tendency that the more loose a company’s WFH policy is, the less monitoring goes on and the
more trust the FTDMs have in their employees.
Moving on, the four key challenges: Customizing work arrangements for employees, Knowledge
sharing is challenged, Hybrid meetings and Reaching out virtually is less natural are used as
a starting point. The aim of the analysis is to gain insights of what lies behind the four key
challenges: Customizing work arrangements for employees, Knowledge sharing is challenged,
Hybrid meetings and Reaching out virtually is less natural. To this end, quotes are used to under-
pin the interpretations leading to formulation of the challenges. Furthermore, other challenges
that relate to the key challenges are also described to gain a more comprehensive understanding
of the underlying aspects of the key challenges. To do this, a further step in the analysis is made
by noting the relations between variables — the variables being the challenges. Herein, all
challenges are taken out of their clusters and instead, the analysis tries to understand how they
are interconnected. This analytic step finds four groups in which the challenges are internally
related. Only two of these groups contain some of the key challenges, and these two are therefore
the only ones to be presented. The rest are presented in Appendix B.4. The relationships between
the challenges are found to understand these more holistically. This will give each challenge
more context and illustrate that the challenges are not isolated. The relatedness also means
that if one challenge is to be solved, others may have to be taken into consideration as well. In
addition to presenting the challenges, an analysis is made of how these are experienced, or not
experienced, depending on the type of HWM for the interviewees. The purpose of this is to dive
deeper into uncover, whether the type of HWM in fact influences the challenges of FTDMs.
The identified relationships resulted in groups, which relate to: Trust and monitoring, Meetings,
knowledge sharing and social factors, Work arrangements and planning and Rules and guidelines.
Moreover, one group includes the challenges, which were not connected with any other group.
Only the groups Meetings, knowledge sharing and social factors and Work arrangements and
planning are presented in the following together with their challenges. Herein, the challenges are
Page 66 of 238
==PAGE:76==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
written with the level 1 code they belong to and the number of interviewees who reported them
in parentheses (like in Table 6). The sub-codes are not included as the interest lies in seeing
when codes from different clusters are connected, and remembering which cluster each sub-code
comes from can be difficult. Hence, the sub-codes are replaced by the level 1 codes to make the
analysis clearer and the conclusions more transparent.
5.2.2
Meetings, knowledge sharing and social factors
This section concerns the challenges related to hybrid meetings, knowledge sharing and social
factors, which were found to be interrelated. This includes the key challenges Knowledge sharing
is challenged, Reaching out is less natural and Hybrid meetings. These challenges and their
interrelations (depicted as arrows) are illustrated in Figure 10. The relationships between the
challenges are influential, which means that challenge A with an arrow pointing to challenge
B may influence the occurrence of B, but A will not necessarily lead B, and A is neither a
precondition for the occurrence of B. Therefore, the relationship marked by arrows illustrates
that one has the ability to influence the occurrence of the other.
Figure 10: Group of interrelated challenges connected to meetings, knowledge sharing and
social factors.
Knowledge sharing is challenged
Beginning with the challenge of knowledge sharing, Figure 10 shows that this is influenced
by four other challenges. Before diving into these relationships, the analysis dives deeper into
Page 67 of 238
==PAGE:77==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
what lies within the challenge of knowledge sharing and whether it is experienced differently
depending on the type of HWM the interviewees have. To do this, quotes from the interview-data
which emphasize the interviewees’ attitudes towards knowledge sharing are drawn out.
Many interviewees comment on the difficulties they find in knowledge sharing due to the fact
that people are not gathered in the office. For instance Int 1, 3 and 5 all believe that knowledge
sharing is done more and better on-site, while Int 6 believes there is a barrier when WFH but this
just needs to be broken down by getting used to the new way of doing things. Int 2 experiences
that silos are being formed within the team and acorss departments due to having a HWM, which
can also harm their knowledge level.
Int 3 talks about how physical presence is necessary for their employees to seek out support, and
that the knowledge sharing between employees is also better on-site from their point of view:
"I also believe it is important that we are present, both to discuss with each other and to have a
talk if they [the employees] are having issues. They don’t just drop by my office when they work
from home. (...) I also think that the informal discussion and knowledge sharing between the
employees work best on-site." 00.03.43
Likewise, Int 5 states that knowledge sharing does not happen outside the office, and that this
is the cost of a HWM, while Int 1 comments on the barrier working from home has, as the
employees cannot just lean over the table and ask a question:
"That possibility for knowledge sharing (...) it is not there, when you work from home. (...)
Collectedly I think it [a HWM] has a cost on the knowledge level." 00.40.53
"(...) when they [the employees] are gathered, it is easier for them to say ’hey, do you have two
minutes?’ (...) and it is much more difficult picking up the phone or writing a chat message."
00.24.01
Int 6 goes in the same direction and also talks about the extra effort it sometimes takes to call
someone up compared to walking up to someone in the office:
"I still think that for many people there are some things that they don’t write or call about. And I
do the same thing myself. (...) I think it is about it just feeling more natural to go talk to someone
in person." 00.34.42
For this challenge, interviewees from all three types of HWMs (OF, OO and RF) experience diffi-
culties. As expected, the challenge is therefore one that does not depend on the type of HWM. In
the above statements, the interviewees express that they feel a barrier against knowledge sharing
in a HWM. The interviewees argue that this is because knowledge sharing feels less natural
from home and that people therefore do not share knowledge across office and home-office. This
connects the challenge of knowledge sharing with one of the other key challenges: Reaching out
virtually is less natural (displayed in the bottom half of Figure 10). The statements especially by
Page 68 of 238
==PAGE:78==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
Int 1 and 6 go into this feeling and place it as a reason why knowledge sharing is challenged in a
HWM. This is why a relationship of influence is indicated between the two challenges in Figure
10.
Reaching out virtually is less natural
Reaching out virtually is less natural is an essential challenge as it is at the center of many others.
This challenge is about managers feeling that it is less natural to call up employees than talking
face to face, and therefore wait until the next time they are at the office to have a meeting. This
aspect is illustrated by Int 1:
"If I have something to talk about and it can wait until the next day, they I would rather wait
until they are in here. (...) I think it is more natural when we sit together." 00.05.57
In addition to the connection to knowledge sharing, the challenge of reaching out is connected
to Social interactions come less natural, Managers perceived as less available and Retaining
management responsibilities. The influence that the challenge of reaching out has on all of these
other challenges is explained in the following. The dotted line in the figure leads to all of the
remaining social challenges, as these have a relationship that begins with Social interactions
come less natural. Thus Reaching out virtually is less natural has an overall influence on all
the other social challenges that arise in a HWM according to the interview-data. The details of
the remaining social factors will not be presented in this analysis, but a figure of their mutual
relationships is included in Appendix B.5.
Int 2 expresses that it feels unnatural to reach out virtually in relation to social interactions:
"’Hey, in two weeks, should we book a time for a cup of coffee virtually on Teams?’ That is a bit
unnatural" 00.09.17
This also leads up to the challenge Social interactions come less natural (displayed in the bottom
left corner of Figure 10).
Social interactions come less natural
This challenge entails a feeling of being less naturally connected socially within the team. Both
Int 2 and 5 express feeling a need to be more explicit in the social interactions because they are
not a natural part of a workday in the hybrid setting:
"When you are at the office, you fall into conversations with people naturally when you go get a
cup of coffee (...). When you sit at home, you have to create these situation yourself and it is a
bit forced." 00.08.58
"We are all dependent on reaching out more explicitly [when WFH]." 00.20.58
Page 69 of 238
==PAGE:79==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
Managers perceived as less available
In addition to the managers themselves feeling that it is less natural to reach out virtually, they
feel the same behavioral pattern from their employees. Int 3 and 6 state that they do not feel their
employees see them as being available from home, which connects the challenge Reaching out
virtually is less natural with Managers perceived as less available, as the former influences the
latter. Int 6 illustrates this point:
"They can always call me — my phone is open most of the day and they can book me for Teams
meetings. (...) I am just as available the days when they telework as when they are on-site. They
just don’t use it." 00.05.00
"People have different opinions on what a manager should be. You need to be available. Well
yes, but I am also available when I work from home — you can just call. It just takes some
getting used to." 00.34.10
Retaining management responsibilities
Out of all the interviewees, Int 6 is the only one who believes that the new way of communication
and sharing knowledge is something that people (including themselves) just have to get used
to, and then it will be the same as interacting face-to-face. Those who refuse to get used to
online interaction keep waiting to discuss things until being face-to-face with others, which Int 6
believes will negatively impact the efficiency when WFH:
"Instead of letting things wait until the next day (...) because then things become so inefficient
because you work from home" 00.15.07 "(...) people just need to get used to making the calls.
Then they will figure out that it is just like walking into an office — it is not dangerous or
different." 00.34.13
In contrast, Int 1, 3, 5 believe that it is better to keep coordination and knowledge sharing for the
times when people are gathered in the office. This point is emphasized by Int 3:
"It is rare that I have something that can’t wait until the next day and then there is no problem.
But if someone was teleworking five days a week it would be different, because then there would
be some things that I would have to deal with also when they were at home." 00.47.31
Int 3 expresses reluctance towards managing employees when they WFH, which results in the
employees being appendices to the team when WFH and only really a part of the team when
being in the office. This symptom is seen in most of the interviews with managers having OF
HWMs, which goes for both Int 1, 3 and 5. There is even a tendency towards giving up their
responsibilities as managers from both Int 1, 3, 5, which connect to the challenge Retaining
management responsibilities from Figure 10 (displayed in the bottom right corner). This means
that when the manager feels that it is less natural to reach out to employees virtually, it can
influence them towards giving up their responsibilities towards the employees:
Page 70 of 238
==PAGE:80==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
"If we do a meeting virtually (...) then I feel, ’OK if you would like to do this virtually then I also
feel that you have the responsibility to make it work’. That way, I will be more active in a
meeting when it is in-person." 00.32.05
"I am not really a manager for the ones working from home." 00.04.31
"When people work from home, I will typically only be in dialogue with them if I have a specific
errand with them. Not to check in and see how things are going"
In this, it is clear that these three managers put a lot of the professional responsibility on their
teleworking employees. So instead of taking charge of the situation and treating employees
equally when WFH and at the office, they create a gap in their tasks and leave people at home
more to their own devices. This could also be connected to their need for monitoring their
employees or their lack of trust in them, as mentioned in the end of Section 5.2.1. The gap that
the managers themselves create could result in a feeling of disconnect, which in turn possibly
decreases their trust in employees or/and increases their need for monitoring.
Human intuition is challenged
Moving on to the top half of Figure 10, there are three challenges influencing the challenge of
knowledge sharing. At the center of this, is Some things not suited for telework, which has a
relationship with Human intuition is challenged. To begin at the root, the analysis first looks at
the challenge regarding the intuition that the interviewees feel they have towards their employees.
The interviewees feel that their Human intuition is challenged by the HWM. This stems from a
feeling of missing connectedness with their employees, which manifests itself differently for
the interviewees. Int 2 talks about how it is difficult to "tune in to" a person on a screen and
to be human, when interacting virtually. Int 5 comments on their complete loss of "feeling in
touch" with the employees, when they telework. Int 4 talks about the importance of being able to
sense what employees are feeling on a more personal level and that this is easier in person. Int
3 expresses difficulties being manager when WFH or when employees WFH due to not being
able to sense what is going on. Lastly, Int 1 comments on generally, when people (including
themselves) work from home "too much", it will make one loose the overview of how people are
doing:
"You loose the overview of what’s going on (...). You can’t get a sense of how things are going.
The social bond is weakened significantly. It is a ’click’ easier to apply for something else."
00.23.00
With this quote, Int 1 also emphasize a relationship between loosing human intuition and both
the challenges of Impairing social connections and Retention — both challenges, which lie
within the Remaining social challenges in the figure, and will not be described in further detail
within this analysis (details are included in Appendix B.5).
Page 71 of 238
==PAGE:81==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
Some things not suited for telework
Due to the interviewees’ feeling of not being able to connect properly with employees on a
personal level when meeting online, there are some thing the interviewees feel are best to do in
person. This links the two challenges Human intuition is challenges and Some things not suited
for telework. The fact that the interviewees feel that their human intuition is weak, influences
their attitudes towards what they feel is suited for telework. This means that there are some
meetings and interactions they prefer to do in person. An aspect that both Int 1, 2, 3 and 4 point
out to be better to do in person are one-on-one meetings, as these are usually about more personal
things regarding the employees. In connection to this Int 4 states:
"Those one-on-one meetings (...) I feel they are in some way just better to do on-location. It has
probably something to do with body language. A feeling of being able to talk about certain
things." 00.07.49
Other aspects which are drawn out as suited best to do in person are: Talking about things that are
a bit out of the ordinary, like making big decisions (Int 4); distributing work tasks (Int 4); giving
praise (Int 2); discussing more "tender" topics (Int 2); being in charge of a meeting (1) or having
informal discussions and knowledge sharing (Int 1 and 3). The quote from Int 2 exemplifies one
of these aspects:
"When we need to talk about things out of the ordinary — it can be positive as well (...) you feel
it in another way, when you are present. So everything that is an ’out of the ordinary meeting’
will be a physical meeting." 00.24.30
Another quote that exemplifies the things that were believed to work best in person is by Int 3
and relates to knowledge sharing, and was also mentioned earlier in relation to why Int 3 believes
that knowledge sharing is challenged in a HWM:
"I also think that the informal discussions and knowledge sharing between the employees works
better on-site." 00.03.55
To wrap up, Interviewee 7 experiences a different dynamic in online or hybrid meetings compared
to in person meetings — decidedly preferring the latter. This is connected to the liveliness of the
design process, and is therefore quite specific to the design profession:
"There are of course totally different dynamics at stake when you are in the same room. You are
able to spontaneously go up to the whiteboard and make drawings (...)" 00.35.39
Interviewees working within an OF model — namely Int 1, 3, 4 and 5 — feel that they loose
human intuition when not being co-located with their employees. The same is the case for one
of the interviewees with an OO model (Int 2). Without their human intuition, these interviewees
feel that there are some things they cannot do virtually — especially things that have a personal
Page 72 of 238
==PAGE:82==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
aspect. In contrast Int 6, who works within a RF model, does not feel that there are some things
that are not suited to do online.
Hybrid meetings
Having gone through the challenges in the top-middle of Figure 10, it is time to move on to
the next. The key challenge regarding Hybrid meetings (displayed in the top right corner of
the figure) entails the difficulties that are inherent to the nature of having a meeting, where
some are present and some are online. Herein all interviewees agree that there is a difference
between hybrid and on-site meetings, and four out of the seven find some of these differences
challenging. As mentioned earlier, Int 7 expresses a preference for physical meetings because
it makes other things possible like drawing on the whiteboard. Int 5 even thinks that meetings
conducted entirely online work better:
"I think that the hybrid format is challenging. In that sense I kind of think it was easier when we
were all working from home or when we are all at work." 00.08.24
Interviewee 2 and 7 argue that they find hybrid meetings more challenging due to the different
dynamics they bring about. According to Int 2 it is more difficult to participate in meetings
when joining online, because it is more difficult being mentally present without the social aspect,
which can make people hide more behind the screen. Int 2 postulate that it is especially difficult
in larger meetings and when needing to coordinate more:
"Of course you can raise your hand in Teams and all that but the dynamic is not just not the
same, so you loose something on the bigger meetings, where you need to coordinate" 00.08.25
Interviewee 1 and 2 talk about the need for breaking a barrier when participating in meetings
online. When being asked whether there is a difference in how teleworkers interact during hybrid
meetings, Int 1 answered:
"Yes, very much. It is a sort of barrier that needs to be broken — especially if you do not have
your webcam on." 00.17.58
Due to this barrier, Int 1 is of the conviction that one needs to be more active when joining a
meeting online:
"I have an expectation that the person working from home is more active. [In regards to] body
language, interpretation, presence — when you telework you lack something, then you need to
compensate. Therefore you need to be more active when you are home." 00.45.05
In conclusion, Int 1, 2, 5, 7 generally experience issues with hybrid meetings connected to
the people joining from home. These include lower mental presence, different dynamics and
Page 73 of 238
==PAGE:83==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
problems with breaking the barrier induced by being on a screen.
Technical limitations
The last challenge in the group connected to Meetings, Knowledge Sharing and Social factors is
Technical limitations. This challenge is only reported by one interviewee, namely Int 5. Int 5’s
team does not have the necessary technical facilities to even conduct hybrid meetings, which is a
big issue, since adequate IT facilities is a precondition to making a HWM work:
"We are not so advanced IT-wise so actually we cannot really conduct hybrid meetings. (...) We
do not really have the necessary facilities to be able to sit together for hybrid meetings."
00.12.06
It is safe to say that not having the necessary facilities will be detrimental for the success
of a HWM, as the inability to conduct the needed work in a way that enables teleworkers to
participate is a precondition for the work model. This point of view is also phrased by Int 6, who
is within an organization with great facilities but acknowledges the importance that this has for
the realization of a functioning HWM:
"It simply requires that the IT follows. There is nothing worse than going into a meeting room
and then things don’t work. That just NEEDS TO work! (...) We are maybe also privileged in the
private industry and then it [IT facilities] is just prioritized. It is simply a necessity if you want
the hybrid management to work. Decent cameras, decent home-offices and those things."
00.07.18
The technical limitations that Int 5 experiences is likely to influence their attitudes towards the
HWM as a whole. It has been illustrated earlier in the analysis, that Int 5 has a predominantly
negative mindset, when it comes to managing a team within a HWM. As an example, recall the
statement:
"If I look at it from a management point of view, I find it difficult to see that there are many
advantages in people working hybrid. But I recognize that it can be convenient for the
individual." 00.37.52
Furthermore, these technical limitations greatly influence the ability that the team has when it
comes to knowledge sharing, which is also indicated with an arrow in Figure 10. In conclusion,
this challenge is not experienced by many of the interviewees, but when it is present, it will have
detrimental ramifications for the knowledge sharing within the team.
From the last challenges in the group of challenges in Figure 10 the analysis shows that all inter-
viewees agree that there is a difference between hybrid and on-site meetings. They have different
reasons for preferring physical meetings including: Different dynamics, lower interactions from
Page 74 of 238
==PAGE:84==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
online participants, the change in a person’s presence when being online and the extra effort it
takes to participate in a meeting from home.
5.2.3
Work arrangements and planning
The last key challenge is Customizing work arrangements for employees, which is displayed
at the top of Figure 11. Below this challenge are two others. These three challenges are gone
trough in the following, beginning with the one at the top.
Figure 11: Group of interrelated challenges connected to Rules and guidelines
Many of the organizations in this study have implemented a type of HWM due to the demand
they feel from their employees. For instance in the organization where Int 1, 2 and 5 work, they
have kept the hybrid work model after COVID-19 due to this demand and to give their employees
the flexibility that a HWM can bring. These points are made clear by Int 1 and 5 respectively
"I think especially it is to accommodate the need that developed during COVID-19 — that you
were able to take an hour and a half out of your calendar and block it. (...) the flexibility that
you got during COVID-19, maintaining that."
"After COVID-19, the demand for telework has been so high that in some way it has become a
competitive parameter — no matter whether our directorate likes it or not." 00.18.07
The quote from Int 5 also shows that nowadays, hybrid work is viewed as a competitive parameter
that organizations need to be able to offer both their current employees and those they wish to
recruit. Int 2 says the same thing:
"It has become a part of their value proposition towards new employees" 00.33.32
Int 4 also establishes hybrid work as a retention tool, as it keeps the employees happy. Moreover,
Int 4 states that some employees view telework more as a right than an added benefit:
Page 75 of 238
==PAGE:85==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
"I think people view it as a great advantage, and after all it is important to keep the employees
happy in these times with workforce shortage." 00.28.36
"I view it as something they feel like is their right and not something I need to give permission
for." 00.13.01
These statements show a pattern of wanting to accommodate the employees. They also show
that the implementation of HWMs in the different organizations, at least to some degree, has
to do with the demand coming from current and future employees. The main thing that all
the interviewees feel they are able to offer their employees from a HWM is flexibility. This is
illustrated in the statement by Int 2, 6 and 7 respectively:
"It is because of the flexibility, I think, that you have happier employees because you don’t need
to structure your life differently and it doesn’t have to be so rigid." 00.16.42
"You win on personal freedom and the possibility to do things in addition to the job as well."
00.08.38
"First of all, it gives a job satisfaction so that the employees feel that they have the freedom in
terms of how they want to live their lives." 00.36.52
In order to uphold this flexibility, the managers need to take into account, the wishes of the
individual employee when arranging the work such that the employees, to some extend, get what
they want. This leads to the first challenge regarding Work Arrangements and Planning, and the
last of the key challenges found in the analysis: Customizing work arrangements for employees.
Customizing work arrangements for employees
This challenge regards the fact that employees have different wishes for their work schedules.
The freedom and choice that a HWM induces, adds another dimension to the DM’s task of
planning. This challenge is mentioned by interviewees from all three types of HWMs, but
it is different what each interviewee emphasize as challenging in the customization of work
arrangements.
Int 2, 3, 5 and 6 all, in different ways, express that it is difficult to accommodate all employees’
wishes regarding their work arrangements. Int 2 puts it quite clearly:
"It is very, very difficult to make a work arrangement that fits all in this area (HWM), so I guess
that two days per week is just fine and then people have to figure it out themselves." 00.15.29
For Int 3 it is about the HWM they have in the organization, and this not pleasing some of the
employees. There are some who feel it is way too little and always challenge the rules:
"There are some who feel that they work from home too rarely and some who would rather be
without days working from home. Those who feel that they do not get to work form home enough
challenge me on the rules to the same extend every time — whether the same rules still apply
and that they really feel that these rules are unreasonable." 00.11.30
Page 76 of 238
==PAGE:86==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
Int 2 and 5 also talk about the individual preferences that play a role when structuring the work
arrangements. Int 5 put is quite generally:
"Some things are important for some and other things are important for others." 00.33.55
Whereas Int 2 goes into more detail about why it is difficult to make work arrangements that
consider all preferences:
"It is very employee-individual and therefore when the organization announce something broad
that I have to channel out to the employees, then I already know who is going to say ’Hmm that
is a bit far out’ and who will say "Yes! That’s awesome!’" 00.13.33
"It is very difficult to make a rule of thumb that sits well with everybody. But you have to do that
to some extend because it is a workplace — people do need to show up in the office sometimes,
but it is very personal what you feel works best so it is really difficult to just cut it and say now it
is just like this for everyone." 00.29.30
Int 7 argues that the needs of the employees and their work arrangements depend on the types of
people and their professional needs:
"I manage a team of designers. They are notoriously known for not liking structure. In contrast,
if you talk to developers, back-end developers for instance, then they would like structure in
their workday and then maybe you as manager need to reach for some other tools." 00.46.28
Another challenging aspect of the HWM in regard to how each individual employee wishes to
structure their work is that in a hybrid setting, people do not just change location, they often
also change working hours. This challenge has two aspects. Int 5 emphasizes the increased
inconvenience it has on their own responsibilities when employees change their working hours:
"When people WFH, at least in my team, it is often combined with them change their work hours
and that gives some challenges. (...) It gives some challenges when we are not only dealing with
the question of physical or virtual presence but also the fact that we are not necessarily at work
at the same time." 00.09.15
This creates a stronger feeling of not being connected with employees and can maybe create a
sense of not being in control for the manager. On the same note, Int 2 enhances the effect that a
HWM can have on the WLB for those WFH as the work and free-time melt together more:
"The nine-to-five work-time crumbles away a bit when people are at home and at five o’clock
you are not off work in the same way because then you have had two hours during the day to do
somethings else (...) and then you put those couple of hours in the evening, and then you get an
email you apply to right away. That way things are moved around because people work in
different ways." 00.09.55
Page 77 of 238
==PAGE:87==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
Int 2 does not phrase the changed working hours as a challenge as such. They mostly see it as
a benefit because it gives the possibility to do some of the things that are usually within one’s
working hours:
"I don’t think we should belittle the value of being able to pick your daughter up from school or
go with your son for scouting or something like tat and then work an hour in the evening."
00.30.09
"It is really healthy to be at home as well. You get to do a lot of other things, maybe push some
work for later in the day, do some laundry, go for a walk, sleep until noon, I don’t know haha —
it doesn’t matter what people do as long as they also do their job." 00.04.49
Along the same lines, Int 6 also sees the ability to change working hours as an opportunity to
increase life quality:
"I myself have two children and then it also just makes a lot of sense for me to be able to put the
hours when I want and then work an hour or two in the evening once in a while if that’s what it
takes to have a less stressful day." 00.16.04
The difference in perception of whether the change in working hours is good or bad from the in-
terviewees’ point of view is also influenced by whether they like to WFH themselves or view this
as challenging. Those who feel like they get increased life quality when WFH tend to perceive
the skewed working hours more as an opportunity for themselves and the employees in stead
of as something that challenges their job as managers. Therefore, the aspect of the challenge,
which regards the chaining of work hours has a tendency to be perceived differently depending
on the type of work model the organization has. This refers back to the characteristics of the
different work models. Recall from Section 2.2.1 where the attitudes towards managers WFH
within the different models were presented based on the work by Griffis (2021). This establishes
that managers in an OF model are usually present in the office, but that on the contrary, managers
from the OO and RF models are not expected to. Therefore, managers within an OF HWM will
not experience the benefits of WFH but will only see the challenges that it brings when their
employees work shifted hours. Consequently, FTDMs with an OF model may be more prone to
feel that it challenges their management role as they lose a sense of being in touch and in control
compared to managers in the other HWMs.
More need for planning
Due to the different preferences of the employees and the different working hours people have in
a HWM, there is an increased need for planning due to the HWM. This connects the challenge
of Customizing work arrangements for employees with More need for planning, as customizing
the schedules creates an increased need for planning. This challenge is only mentioned by
one interviewee, namely Int 2, but this may be due to the novelty of the managers who were
Page 78 of 238
==PAGE:88==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
interviewed. They are all first-time managers and have a relatively short management tenure
and all of them (except Int 5) have only been managers for hybrid teams, and have therefore
not something to compare the amount of planning needed for the HWM with. The challenge
mentioned by Int 2 in regards to planning entails a difficulty when scheduling in-office days and
meetings — especially due to the fact that people do not necessarily work during the regular
working-hours because they have private appointments instead:
"I think that there is more planning now after COVID-19 and after hybrid management is a
reality. We need to plan more to make it all work. You need to plan a bit more to make it all work
out. And then there is the thing about scheduling meetings and figuring out who is in the office
and when, and ’Can you participate?’, ’No, I am seeing my physiotherapist’, ’Ah, well, OK
then’... That kind of thing." 00.53.04
This increased need for planning that Int 2 talks about is related to the last challenge from Figure
11: Overbooked calendars.
Overbooked calendars
Int 2 and 7 both talk about a tendency they have experienced in the hybrid teams, where
everything is booked as a meeting — also small things that should just take two minutes to talk
about. It is described Int 2 in the following statement:
"The calendars have been blocked up and the meeting culture has gone crazy. So many of the
little things have become half an hour. (...) There is a tendency towards people turning things,
which should not have been a meeting, into a meeting." 00.37.19
This makes it difficult to get a hold of people with short notice and according to Int 7:
".. it takes away a certain spontaneity." 00.17.15
Int 2 goes on to explain how the evolution of the meeting culture affects the ability to get a hold
of people:
"When you look at people’s calendars now, they are blocked all day with meetings all the way
through. (...) Consequently, if I need to book someone for something, I sometimes need to look
two weeks into the future before I find a spare moment, and I can’t just go ask." 00.36.59
This indicates an influence between overbooked calendars and knowledge sharing, as the in-
ability to catch people for a quick chat slows down the process of and decreases the possibility
for knowledge sharing. The relationship between the challenges of planning and overbooked
calendars goes both ways. The increased need for planning makes people set up more meetings
and due to the many meetings, people need to do even more planning to find time to meet. These
two challenges are only mentioned by managers from the OO work model, which indicates that
Page 79 of 238
==PAGE:89==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
having a structure that resembles being half at home, more meetings are conducted.
Summary
Summing up the findings connected to the challenges in Figure 10, knowledge sharing is a
challenge within any HWM due to people — managers as well as employees — feeling that it is
less natural to reach out virtually than when being co-located. Instead of getting used to the new
way of communicating, the interviewees with an OF HWM choose to ignore the fact that they
need to do some things online that they feel are easier to do in person — ultimately giving up
their responsibilities as managers for those who telework. This reaction creates a gap between
the managers and their teleworking employees, potentially pushing these managers to trust less
in their employees or feel a higher need for monitoring.
Within the OO and RF work models, the analysis shows that interviewees had a mindset that
favors the hybrid work arrangement. The interviewee with a RF work model views sharing
knowledge remotely as something that is new and therefore people need to get used to it. In
contrast, the analysis found that interviewees within an OF work model generally had a more
negative attitude towards hybrid work due to the difficulties in knowledge sharing and virtual
interactions. The interviewees within an OO model favor knowledge sharing on-location but
they are not opposed to the virtual format like the analysis suggests the FTDMs within the OF
model to be.
Lastly, the analysis finds that having a HWM but not having adequate technical facilities to
conduct hybrid meetings creates a very difficult situation for the FTDM. Fortunately, only one
interviewee reported to experience this challenge.
Summing up the findings connected to the challenges in Figure 11, interviewees from all types of
HWMs wish to give the desired flexibility to their employees. This creates a need for customizing
the work arrangements for their employees which increases the complexity of the FTDM-role
and adds another dimension to their task of planning. On the other hand, it is a part of the tasks for
the FTDM to make these arrangements and structure the daily work for the employees. The work
arrangements must be in accordance with the company policy for a HWM, and sometimes this
puts the FTDM in a difficult spot when employees and upper-management are in disagreement.
In connection to making customized work arrangements, FTDMs may experience that their
employees do not only change their location when WFH but also changes their working hours.
This challenges especially the interviewees from the OF work model, as they are not able to
experience the positive effects of changing the working hours themselves but only see the
challenges that it brings when their employees work shifted hours. In contrast, interviewees
from the OO and RF work models often WFH themselves and achieve great benefits from this
in terms of WLB and personal freedom, which makes them view the possibility of changing
Page 80 of 238
==PAGE:90==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
working hours as more a benefit than a challenge.
In conclusion for the analysis of the key challenges and those connected to them, there seems to
be something to be said generally about the challenges FTDMs experience depending on what
HWM they work within.
The analysis shows that many challenges are only experienced by FTDMs with an OF work
model, fewer but still a large portion are also experienced by FTDMs working within an OO
work model and that quite few are experienced by FTDMs working within a RF work model.
The challenges only experienced by FTDMs from the OF model could be caused by the approach
these managers have toward WFH. Finally, for the HWM to thrive, a FTDM should have a
mindset of treating teleworking employees equally to employees working on-site.
5.3
Choosing which challenge to solve
Having gone through the analysis of the key challenges and those challenges connected to them,
the time has come to determine which of the key challenges to address in the solution proposal.
This is evaluated based on the previous analytical findings from the content-analytic meta matrix
and the causal networks.
When choosing the most crucial challenge before diving into a solution proposal, it is important
to take multiple factors into consideration. In the following, relevant aspects for determining
the importance/significance of each of the key challenges are examined — one challenge at a
time. This is to present the findings from the previous analysis, which influence the impact of a
potential solution for the four key challenges.
Generally, when solving challenges within an organization, it is important to think about the
actors connected to the challenge. Who is affected by the challenge? Who has the power and
responsibility to do something about it? It is obvious from the the scope of the conducted
interviews and following analysis, that the ones affected by the challenges are the managers,
as these are extracted directly from the statements of the managers themselves. Additionally,
many of these challenges end up indirectly hurting either the employees, the organization or
both because of the difficulties the FTDMs face in executing their responsibilities. Whichever
challenge is solved will relieve the FTDM of some part of the struggle that lies in managing
a hybrid team, and can thereby indirectly help the employees, as they will have a DM, who is
better equipped to take on the role. This may in turn create a better team with more adequate
skills and capacity to perform their duties, thus being capable of creating more value for the
company.
It has already been established that many FTDMs are not prepared for the task ahead of them
due to their lack of training in obtaining managerial qualifications. Choosing a challenge, which
Page 81 of 238
==PAGE:91==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
would be the responsibility of the FTDM to resolve, would be putting even more pressure on
them than what already exists. Therefore, choosing an organizational challenge (recall the cate-
gorization from Table 6) would put the responsibility onto the organization in stead of loading
even more onto the FTDM’s plate. Only the key challenges from the analysis, which have been
categorized as organizational challenges will be considered for further investigation. These are
Customizing work arrangements for employees and Knowledge sharing is challenged.
Moving on with the two challenges from above, another aspect which should be considered
before writing up a solution to any problem is the connection between challenges. Generally,
when solving challenges within an organization, it is important to begin at the root of the problem.
If a solution is found for an isolated issue, but the cause of the issue is not addressed, then the
proposed solution will most likely only be treating the symptoms of the larger, underlying issue.
Therefore, when choosing challenges to solve, organizations must be aware of how these are
connected and attempt to tackle the root causes to achieve the largest effect/highest impact.
For the key challenges, this aspects is illustrated in the causal networks in Figure 10 and 11,
where the inter-dependencies are presented. In these figures, the arrows illustrate an influencing
relationship between two challenges. Thus, the challenges that have an arrow pointing towards
them are underlying challenges of others. This makes them symptoms of a higher level challenge
and thereby disqualifies them for selection. Looking back at the causal networks from the
analysis, it is apparent that the key challenge Knowledge sharing is challenged is at the center
of many others. It is influenced by four other challenges — one of which is influenced by yet
another challenge. This means, that tackling Knowledge sharing is challenged would not be
possible whiteout taking into account all of the surrounding challenges. On the other hand, the
key challenge Customizing work arrangements for employees seems to be the root cause of other
challenges, and not being influenced by any of the other challenges found in the analysis.
In conclusion, the challenge: Customizing work arrangements for employees is chosen, and a
solution proposal is given in the following section.
Spradlin (2012) establishes important steps for preparing a solution for an organizational chal-
lenge. These include the following four points, which are studied to prepare for the solution.
1) Defining the problem and scope and formulating it clearly
Employees in knowledge-based organizations have different wishes and expectations for their
work arrangements when it comes to telework. Concurrently, most organizations have made
general guidelines for how often they wish for their workers to be in the office versus WFH.
These two do not always come together, and even when they do, it is difficult for FTDMs to make
work arrangements that accommodate the flexibility and changing working hours that come
with hybrid work. The FTDMs are often caught in the middle of company lines and employee
demands, where the freedom and choice that a HWM induces, and the vague guidelines from
Page 82 of 238
==PAGE:92==
5
QUALITATIVE ANALYSIS
Master Thesis
the organizations, add another dimension to the FTDM’s task of planning.
The scope of this challenge is within the individual teams where the DM is a FTDM.
2) Establishing the desired outcome
The desired outcome of the proposed solution is a management tool that can help FTDMs in
decision making when planning the work arrangements for a hybrid team. Ideally, this tool will
be able to: take relevant, individual preferences towards telework into account; be easy to use
for untrained DMs who can have multiple types of backgrounds; relieve FTDMs of the chosen
challenge without creating any added issues that will complicate their work unnecessarily; and
be able to operate within organizations with different types of HWMs.
To achieve the desired outcomes, it is interesting to use a prediction model for creating the tool.
A benefit of quantitative methods like a prediction model is that they can generate predictions
based on immense amounts of data, thus giving insights into an area involving many people.
Moreover, organizations are to an increasing extend interested in incorporating elements like
business-intelligence into their operations. This provides an incentive to create a data-based
prediction model, such that organizations have an interest in implementing the tool into their
businesses. The challenge is to find a way to accommodate the individual expectations from
employees in a way that is clear for the FTDM. Having large amounts of data on people, which
contain information on the aspects that are influential for determining how much people wish
to WFH, gives a foundation to predict their preferred number of teleworking days. Thus, a
predictive model has the potential of providing a tool to use resolve the chosen challenge.
3) What resources may the solution require? Who has the responsibility of carrying it out?
Assuming that a solution is found, its implementation in organizations may take a bit of work.
It requires that an organization takes a high-level conversation about allocating the needed
resources. It takes large amounts of data collected across the organization, which is a decision
that needs to be made from the top. In this conversation it makes sense to include human resource
managers, as HR would be left with the responsibility of making sure the FTDMs are provided
with the proper training to use the tool as an integrated part of their work.
Having chosen a challenge to solve, described this, established the desired outcome of a solution
and identified who has the responsibility of carrying out such a solution, it is time to dive into
actually creating the solution for the chosen challenge. This is done in the following section.
Page 83 of 238
==PAGE:93==
6
PROPOSED SOLUTION TOOL: A PREDICTIVE MODEL
Master Thesis
6
Proposed solution tool: A predictive model
In this section, second part of research question 3 is addressed
Which of the identified key challenges makes sense for knowledge-based organizations to solve?
And can this challenge be solved using a predictive model based on the data from Epinion?
The answer to which challenge that makes sense to solve, was answered at the end of the previous
section. Here it was found that the challenge: Customizing work arrangements for employees, is
the key challenge that makes sense to solve. From here, the second part of the research question
is addressed.
When the FTDMs need to make work arrangements that fit with both the organizational guide-
lines and the personal preferences of the employees, they first and foremost need to establish
which organizational lines to follow and what each employee sees as their preferred WFH
schedule. The solution proposal made using a predictive model will address the second aspect
— namely figuring out an ideal way to establish the preferences of employees, based on rele-
vant parameters. A simple way of doing this, would be to ask the employees how many days
per week they wish to WFH. However, this may result in a number of days, which would be
convenient for the employee, but not necessarily ideal for neither themselves, the team or the
organization. To take an example; a person who wishes to WFH to be able to do practical things
(like doing laundry) during work hours or because they feel that it is more convenient to stay
home, may say that they wish to WFH the majority of the week. But this may both decrease
their professional output as well as harm their social connectivity towards their team, because
the "preference" greatly favors convenience. This issue was stressed in the qualitative analysis
by some interviewees, who felt that sometimes employees were not equipped to handle their
own hybrid schedule, which would result in the challenge The comfort of WFH is unhealthy for
some. In order to avoid this challenge as a result of customizing work arrangements, a procedure
must be set in place, which is able to hinder personal preferences from harming employees and
their performance when customizing their work arrangements. To this end, machine learning
(ML) is used. In this, a specific selected number of features, that are more or most relevant to the
target variable are used in a ML algorithm, that predicts the number of days an employee should
WFH, due to their feelings on the relevant features. This way the customization for the work
arrangements of employees is based on statistical grounds from a large number of respondents
from the data on which the model is built.
To get organizations on board with giving more of the decision capacity to FTDMs, when
making hybrid work arrangements, upper-management needs to feel that the basis of the work
arrangements and the number of days employees are allowed to WFH are founded in relevant
features that prioritize the work more than the individual. The fact that machine learning model
will have a statistical foundation and consider features relevant for the employees’ working
Page 84 of 238
==PAGE:94==
6
PROPOSED SOLUTION TOOL: A PREDICTIVE MODEL
Master Thesis
conditions rather than their individual convenience, may persuade organizations with strict
WFH policies to be more willing to accommodate employees preferences. This is an essential
part of the solution proposal as well, since the challenge can only be solved if employees and
organizations are able to find a common ground for the HWM.
Based on these considerations regarding the problem and potential solution, data collected by
Epinion is used for the machine learning model that aims to predict the number of days per week
employees in KBOs should WFH. To do this, the following chapter will be structured as the
CRISP-DM framework proposes, by first getting an Business understanding of the problem to
solve. Then investigating the data from Epinion in Data Understanding, preparing the data for
modeling in Data Preparation, then modeling in Modeling and finally evaluating the models in
Evaluation. The Deployment phase will be an integrated part of the discussion and is therefore
not present in this chapter. Finally, it is evaluated whether the final ML model would be able to
solve the challenge of Customizing work arrangements for employees.
6.1
Business Understanding
The first phase of the CRISP-DM framework, is to get an understanding of the business involved
in the solution, and setting up success criteria of the project. In the previous section, Section 5.3,
much of the background was already established as well as the value the solution can provide
for the FTDMs. In the previous section the challenge to try solve was identified, the business
scope was defined, and the resources needed and the desired outcome was also identified. Since
these aspects crosses some of the first steps in Business Understanding, this section will go into
details how the business success criteria are related with the success criteria for the data mining
problem.
The stated success criteria for the business aspects is developing a data driven decision tool,
that can be used by FTDMs to structure employees of knowledge work’s work arrangement in
terms of working hybrid. In cooperation with the next phase of the CRISP-DM framework, Data
Understanding, the data is inspected to establish how this tool can be constructed in practice.
The data has not been collected for this purpose, and therefore it is crucial to investigate the
data before any solution methodology can be determined. In the process of identifying how the
quantitative data can solve the selected challenge, machine learning will be used to predict the
following target variable: ’From now on, if your workplace allows it, how many days per week
would you like to work from home?’.
As the goal is to create a decision tool for FTDMs to use, it is crucial that the predictive model
generate results that can be trusted to reflect the preferences of the hybrid team’s working
arrangement. Therefore, the data mining success criteria is to develop a machine learning
algorithm that only produces a few misclassifications of the respondents preferred number of
Page 85 of 238
==PAGE:95==
6
PROPOSED SOLUTION TOOL: A PREDICTIVE MODEL
Master Thesis
days WFH. A specific threshold for the accuracy score will not be set, nor will the number of
misclassifications, as there would be no argumentation of choosing a specific numeric threshold
based on literature. It is very subject to the problem. Nonetheless, the numeric results will be
evaluated and later discussed in terms of the usefulness in practice.
The overall assumption of a functioning solution tool is that, the organizations implementing
this tool are willing to give up the control of structuring the work arrangement of the employees
within the organization. Thus, organizations must trust that the information from the decision
tool will enable the FTDM to create a working arrangement that will accommodate the individual
employee as well as the needs within the team, resulting in a high functioning team. Creating
better conditions for the individual as well as the team will increase their ability to generate
value, thus creating benefit for the organization as a whole.
With this decision tool, one of the key challenges of FTDM experience will be solved, which in
the end will create great value for the individual FTDM and the organization.
6.2
Data Understanding
Having established the business aspect of the project; understanding how the problem relates to
the obstacles observed in literature and in interviews, the data is now investigated.
The secondary data consists of six dataframes, each representing a different data gathering
period, which were merged into one data frame. A timeline of the six data gathering periods,
and the COVID-19 characteristics of each period are displayed in Appendix C.1 (Kirchner
et al., 2022). As mentioned in Section 3.9, the survey for the secondary data consists of 23
questions asking about the respondents’ advantages and disadvantages when WFH, the use of
communication tools and demographic variables. The survey also asked open questions, but
these are not included, as the models used in this thesis cannot handle this type of data.
The secondary data consists of 86 variables and 8,175 observations, where each observation
describes an individual’s answer in that time period. This means that the same person could
potentially have answered the survey all six time periods, thus creating six different observations.
It is however not possible to tell this information from the data, which is why these possible
cases are not handled. This creates the assumption that all observations are unique.

Even though data exclusion often takes place in the next phase, Data Preparation, some variables
will be excluded in this phase, as the perspective of this analysis has already been outlined.
The analysis needs to reflect a general work situation, for people who have the opportunity to
WFH, and are employees of FTDMs. For this reason, the managers are excluded as the proposed
solution is a tool for FTDMs to use on employees, and the observations should therefore only
reflect the employees. The non-knowledge workers are removed, as the study is limited to
investigate knowledge workers. The COVID-19 related variables are excluded as they picture a
moment in time, and not necessarily general preferences. The removed variables are collected in
Appendix C.2.
The process of removing the variables and observations was (1) Displaying all variables, (2)
removing irrelevant survey data. (3) Removing all missing values in target variable (From now
on, if your workplace allows it, how many days per week would you like to work from home?).
(4) Removing all observations before august 2021, ending up with data from two data-collection
points: August and November 2021, since 100% of the knowledge workers were allowed back at
the office (Kirchner et al., 2022). (5) Removing all COVID-19 related variables. (6) Removing
observations and variables of managers and non-knowledge workers. In the end, the data con-
sisted of 60 variables and 1.429 observations. In Appendix C.3, a table of all the kept variables
are displayed together with the variable type and their minimum and maximum value, or the
number of different categories within.
Table 10 shows a simple overview of the variables, describing the topic they relate to and
the number of variables in that topic. The demographic variables describes the gender, age,
occupation, education and number of young people and adults present at home when WFH.
The variables describing both challenges and benefits of WFH entail a series of questions of
which the respondents could rate on a range of one to five how much the topic of the question
affected them. The subject of the questions cover a wide field. Some examples are questions
asking about transportation time, the work environment, social interactions, productivity, etc..
The variables describing the used technology the respondents should specify which technical
tools they use when WFH. The variables that are describing the work load while WFH, are
also a series of questions as the benefits of WFH and challenges of WFH variables. For the two
variables describing social interactions, the respondent were asked to state the number of people
they were in contact with when WFH and how WFH affects their relationship with co-workers.
The two variables with survey information are the month and year and the respondents ID. The
last variable in the table is the target variable.

6.3
Data Preparation
In this section, a few more variables are removed, the categorical variables will either be one-hot
encoded or integer coded9. Then, it will be investigated how many features to include in the
model using the Recursive Feature Elimination (RFE) which shows the accuracy and number of
features. Finally, by evaluating the number of features to use, the features are extracted using the
RFE.
Further removal of variables
Since almost all irrelevant variables of the project scope have been removed, only a few variables
need to be excluded. This involves all open-ended question variables, as the ML models cannot
take these as input. The removed variables are listed in Appendix C.2.
Integer and one-hot encoding
The two models, RF Classifier and NB Classifier can both handle categorical variables as they
are without one-hot encoding. However, before creating the models, the RFE is used, and this
algorithm cannot handle this type of variable. Therefore the following variables are one-hot
encoded: ’To which gender identity do you most identify?’, ’What is your age?’, ’What is the
highest level of education you have achieved?’ and ’From now on, if your workplace allows it,
how many days per week would you like to work from home?’. In the gender variable, the obser-
vations with the values ’Other’ and ’Prefer not to say’ are removed, as only three observation are
present.
The variables concerning the systems the respondents use are integer coded. These variables can
be integer coded as they have a natural order, that can be converted numerically. The question
could for example have been: ’On a scale from one to five, how much do you use this system’,
and the question would be presented on a measurable numeric scale as follows: 5 (Always), 4
(Often), 3 (Sometimes), 2 (Rarely), 1 (Never). The final data used in feature selection consists of
1056 observations from knowledge workers and 59 variables, with one being the target variable.
Feature Selection
The feature selection, RFE, will through recursive training eliminate the least important feature
at a time, until the given number of preferred features is reached. By doing so, the approach
removes the redundant and irrelevant features, and only keeps the variables significant to the
target variable. In this way, the prediction power of the algorithms increases and the risk of
over-fitting the models decreases.
9An approach to deal with categorical data, that has a natural ordered relationship; it can increase or decrease
numerically
Page 94 of 238
==PAGE:104==
6
PROPOSED SOLUTION TOOL: A PREDICTIVE MODEL
Master Thesis
Before running RFE, the number of features that should be used, is investigated. Figure 18
visualizes the accuracy of including different numbers of features. After approximately six
features, the accuracy reaches a small peak, and oscillates for each extra feature. This might be
caused by the fact that when adding the extra feature, it includes data that contradicts the pattern
found by the other features.
Figure 18: Number of features and their accuracy
From what is observed in Figure 18, the highest accuracy score is achieved with 48 features,
which is marked with a dotted line in the figure. Using 48 features would not guarantee a much
higher accuracy compared to the large number of extra features to include, and this would result
in an over-fitted model. Therefore, as the first peak in accuracy happens using six features, six
features are included in the models.
The RFE estimator chooses the following six features to include in the model:
• To what extent do you agree or disagree that the following factors benefit your current
working from home? [I like the atmosphere in my home better than at work]
• To what extent do you agree or disagree that the following factors hinder your current
working from home? [I do not get to see my colleges or other people as much as I
would have liked to]
• What kind of work are you doing at the moment? [I am teaching and supervising]
• What kind of work are you doing at the moment? [I work with creative productions or
products]
• 21 to 30 years
• Above 60 years

6.4
Modeling
This section, presents the modeling of the Random Forest Classifier and Naive Bayes Classifier.
For each Classifier, a new model will be generated with over-sampled training data. It is explained
how the training data is over-sampled and how the models, Random Forest (RF) Classifier and
Naive Bayes (NB) Classifier, are built. The fundamentals of the algorithm is not described
here, as this was done in the literature review. For all RF Classifiers, hyper parameter tuning is
implemented to optimize the performance of the model. This is not done for the Naive Bayes
Classifier, as the algorithm does not allow this.
It was observed in the descriptive analysis, that the target variable was imbalanced, which is why
a new model for each Classifier is build with over-sampled data. This is to investigate how the
numeric results differ when balancing the data, and to avoid the model predicting towards the
large class, Class 2, only because this class contains the majority of the observations.
Since the over-sampling algorithm matches the number of observations in the largest class, the
final number of observations in each class in the training set is 264 observations.
Random Forest Classifier
The Random Forest Classifier, consists of a list of parameters that can be varied. In order to build
the most robust and accurate model, hyper parameter tuning is performed before generating the
model. By hyper parameter tuning, the algorithm ends up finding the most optimal combination
of the parameters within the model. An example of a parameter in Random Forest Classifier is
’max_depth’ (maximum depth of tree), that controls maximum number of levels in each decision
tree. In practice, the hyper parameter tuning algorithm has a list of numbers being the max_depth,
and then the tuning chooses the optimal maximum depth of the tree. In Appendix C.4, all the
different parameters that were investigated are shown as well as their definition.
Table 12 shows the final optimal parameter of the Random Forest Classifier.
Table 12: Parameters of Random Forest Classifier
Parameter
Value
Definition

Naive Bayes Classifier
Implementing the Naive Bayes Classifier is quite straightforward, as it is built upon probabilities,
and therefore almost no parameters can vary in the model. The only parameter that can be
implemented, is prior probabilities, which are probabilities of an event before the data was
Page 96 of 238
==PAGE:106==
6
PROPOSED SOLUTION TOOL: A PREDICTIVE MODEL
Master Thesis
collected. Since no probabilities of the data was known before collection, hyper parameter tuning
is not possible for this algorithm.
6.5
Evaluation
In the following section, the two machine learning models are evaluated on the metrics described
in Section 2.7 and compared to the models with over-sampled data. When the models have been
evaluated, one model is selected to improve and the following steps in the CRISP-DM model,
are revisited: Business Understand, Data preparation, Modeling and Evaluation.
Table 13 shows the accuracy of the two models (after hyper parameter tuning), with and without
over-sampling for the the two models. Of the two models with the original data, Random Forest
Classifier estimates the highest accuracy on 0.41, followed by Naive Bayes with an accuracy
on 0.35. The accuracy for the models with the over-sampled data are 0.28 for RF and 0.24 for
NB, which is 0.13 lower than the accuracy for the models with the original data. Therefore,
considering only the accuracy, the models with the original data would be the preferred models.
The accuracy for the models with the original data, however, do not take the imbalanced classes
into account. Therefore, to assess the models with and without oversampling, their Confusion
Matrices and F1 Scores are essential to look into, as these create a more detailed picture of how
well the models perform within each class.

struggles to predict the classes 0 days and 4 days, since there is no correct predictions for these
classes (marked with red in the table). This means that the algorithm cannot find patterns in the
data that distinguish these two classes from the rest of the classes. For all the actual classes, the
RF Classifier predicts the highest number of observations to belong in Class 2. This confirms
that the model contains bias towards Class 2, which also explains the higher accuracy for the
model with the original data.
The predicted observations in the Confusion Matrix for RF Classifier with over-sampled data are
much more scattered across the different classes. The predictions are not skewed towards Class

the model with the original data is classifying more predictions correctly than the model with

the imbalanced data, the model had a tendency to classify most of the predictions in Class 2.
Therefore, even though the accuracy for this class and the overall accuracy have decreased for
the model with over-sampling, the overall performance of predicting all classes has increased as
this model does not classify most of the observations in Class 2. The balanced data ensures the
model is predicting based on the patterns the data contain rather than what most people have
answered.

pattern was observed for the RF Classifier.
